Defending the Verbal Definition Against Objections
Author argues verbal definition is a conceptual demand, not a judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that verbal definition remains a conceptual demand despite objections about mental existence and the unity of knowledge.

Core Idea
The phantom is necessary for disclosure but is not itself knowledge.

The author faces two objections: one from proofs of mental existence, and another from the unity of knower and known. He responds by clarifying that mental existence proves things exist in the mind, not that the image is identical to knowledge. He also defends combining the phantom and image doctrines, so long as they are not both considered knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Find the objection that proofs of mental existence might undermine the conceptual nature of definition.
The objection: 'فما تصنع لدلائل الوجود الذهني؟'
2
Analyze the author's reply
He says those proofs only establish mental existence, not that it is the same as knowledge. This preserves the distinction between concept and judgment.
Reply: 'لو سلمت فلا تنتج إلا أن للأشياء وجودا ذهنيا، لا أنه هو'
3
Identify the second objection
The objector accuses him of combining two contradictory doctrines: the phantom and the occurrence of the image.
Objection: 'هذا جمع بين المذهبين مذهب الشبح ومذهب حصول الصورة'
4
Analyze the reply
The author says this combination is only corrupt if both are considered knowledge and principles of disclosure. But he holds that the known is disclosed only by the phantom, and the image is not knowledge but a necessary relation of knowledge.
Reply: 'إنما الفساد لو قيل إن الشبح والصورة كلاهما علمان... ونحن لا نقول به'

Verbal Definition: Conceptual or Assertive Demand?
Author argues whether verbal definition is conceptual or assertive and resolves differing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given instance of verbal definition is a conceptual demand or an assertive demand based on the definer's intention and the context.

Core Idea
Verbal definition is conceptual in essence, but can function as assertive depending on the definer's intention.

When we define a word by providing a more familiar synonym, are we merely presenting a concept, or are we making a judgment that the word signifies that concept? This passage from Bahr al-Ulum tackles precisely this question. It distinguishes between two cognitive demands—conceptual (tasawwur) and assertive (tasdiq)—and argues that verbal definition belongs primarily to the conceptual domain, though in some contexts it can involve assent. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly interpreting definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demand
Read the opening: 'The second: The verbal definition is from the conceptual demands.' The author places verbal definition under conceptual demands because it answers 'what is it?' by presenting an image, not a judgment.
In Islamic logic, tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) are the two fundamental cognitive achievements.
2
Analyze the objection and response about presence
The author raises an objection: if the listener already has the concept (e.g., lion), explaining 'al-ghadanfar means lion' seems redundant. The response is that the definition presents the meaning specifically from the perspective of being the signification (madlul) of the word, which is a new aspect not previously present.
This distinction uses the concept of haythiyya (perspective) to resolve the problem of presenting what is already present.
3
Contrast verbal definition with linguistic investigation
The author explicitly distinguishes verbal definition from demonstrating the conventionality of a word (wad' al-lafz). The latter is an assertive demand because it requires proof that a word is used for a meaning; verbal definition merely explains the meaning.
Some scholars conflated the two, leading to the mistaken view that all verbal definitions are assertive.
4
Apply the resolution based on intention
The author concludes that if the listener is attentive and the concept is present, verbal definition serves to confirm the word-meaning relation (assent). If the listener is heedless, it is conceptual. The definer's intention determines the nature.
Thus, the same verbal definition can be either conceptual or assertive depending on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي: التَّعْرِيفُ اللَّفْظِيُّ منَ المطالب التصورية ؛ فَإِنَّهُ جواب مَا. ألا ترى إذا قلنا الغضنفر موجود. فقال مَا هُوَ جَوَابُ مَا فَهُوَ تَصَوُّرٌ. الْمُخَاطَبُ مَا الْغَضَفَرُ ؟ فَفَسَّرْنَاهُ بِالْأسد، فليس هناك حكم. بل إحضار صورة.
فإن قلت : المطالب التصورية يطلب بها صورة غير حاصلة ؟ قلت: بل يطلب الأعم منه ومن الإحضار، فتأمل فيه .
فإن قلت: إذا خوطب من حضر عنده صورة الأسد. سد، وقيل الغضنفر موجود وفسر الغضنفر يلزم إحضار الحاضر. قلت الغرض من التعريف اللفظي إحضار المعنى من حيث إنه مدلول اللفظ ، وبهذه الحيثية غير حاضر ، ولا يذهب عليك أنه على هذا التقدير يلزم تحصيل أمر لم يكن حاصلا من قبل ؛ فإن المعنى من حيث المدلولية لم يكن حاصلا قط، وإن جعل الحيثية تعليلية خارجة عن المطلوب يلزم إحضار الحاضر قطعا.
نَعَمْ، بَيَانُ مَوْضُوعِيَّةِ اللَّفْظِ فِي جَوَاب هَلْ هَذَا اللَّفْظُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى بَحْتُ لَفْظِيٌّ، يُقْصَدُ إِثْبَاتُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فِي عِلْمِ اللُّغَةِ))، فهو من المطالب التصديقية، (فَمَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ لَمْ يُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَهُ)) أي بين التعريف اللفظي (وَبَيْنَ الْبَحْثِ اللَّفْظِيُّ اللُّغَوِيِ). القائل السيد المحقق، والتحقيق في هذا المقام أنه إذا عرفت بالتعريف اللفظي عند حضور المعرف والتفاته لا يكون المقصود منه إلا التصديق، وإذا لم يكن ملتفتا بل ذهل عنه وكان موجودا في الخزانة فيحتمل الأمرين، فالمدار على قصد المعرف، فتأمل.
The second: The verbal definition is from the conceptual demands; for it is a response to 'what is it?' Do you not see that if we say 'al-ghadanfar exists,' then he says 'what is it?' it is a response to 'what,' so it is a concept. The addressee [asks] 'what is al-ghadanfar?' So we explain it as 'the lion,' so there is no judgment, rather a presentation of an image. If you say: Conceptual demands seek an image that is not obtained? I say: Rather it seeks what is more general than that and presentation, so reflect on it. If you say: If one is addressed who has the image of the lion already present, and it is said 'al-ghadanfar exists' and al-ghadanfar is explained, then it necessitates presenting what is already present. I say: The purpose of verbal definition is to present the meaning from the perspective that it is the signification of the word, and from this perspective it is not present. And it is not hidden from you that on this estimation it necessitates obtaining something that was not obtained before; for the meaning from the perspective of being signification was never obtained. And if the perspective is made explanatory outside the demanded, it necessitates presenting what is present definitively. Yes, the demonstration of the conventionality of the word in answering 'Is this word conventionally used for a meaning?' is a verbal linguistic investigation, intended to be proven by evidence in linguistics, so it is from the assertive demands. So whoever said that it is from the assertive demands did not differentiate between it, i.e., between verbal definition and the verbal linguistic investigation. The proponent is al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq. And the verification in this place is that if you define by verbal definition while the defined is present and the addressee attentive, then the intention is nothing but assent; and if he is not attentive but heedless and it exists in his treasury, then it permits both possibilities. So the criterion is the intention of the definer, so reflect.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and whether it belongs to conceptual demands (al-matalib al-tasawwuriyya) or assertive demands (al-matalib al-tasdiqiyya). The author begins by stating the position that verbal definition is conceptual because it answers the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?) by providing an image, not a judgment. For example, when one explains that 'al-ghadanfar' means 'lion', no judgment is made; only a conceptual image is presented. The author then anticipates an objection: if verbal definition is conceptual, it should only be needed when the image is not yet present. But if the listener already has the image of a lion, then explaining 'al-ghadanfar' as 'lion' would be presenting what is already present, which seems pointless. The author responds that the purpose is to present the meaning specifically from the perspective of being the signification (madlul) of the word, which is a new aspect not previously present. Even if the concept of lion is known, the fact that the word 'al-ghadanfar' signifies that concept is not known, so something new is acquired. The author then distinguishes verbal definition from a related but distinct inquiry: the demonstration of a word's conventionality (wad' al-lafz), which is a linguistic investigation that seeks to prove that a word is conventionally used for a meaning. This is properly an assertive demand because it requires proof. Some scholars, like al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq, mistakenly conflated this with verbal definition, treating verbal definition as purely assertive. The author resolves the dispute by noting that the nature of verbal definition depends on the context: if the listener is attentive and the concept is already present, the definition serves to confirm the relation between word and meaning, which is an act of assent. If the listener is heedless and the concept is latent, the definition serves to bring the concept to mind, which is conceptual. Thus, the definer's intention determines whether the definition is conceptual or assertive.

Key Terms
التعريف اللفظي
المطالب التصورية
المطالب التصديقية
المدلول
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and linguistic investigation (al-bahth al-lughawi)?
A) Verbal definition is always conceptual, while linguistic investigation is always assertive.
B) Verbal definition answers 'what is it?' by providing an image, while linguistic investigation answers 'Is this word used for this meaning?' and requires proof.
C) Verbal definition is restricted to Arabic, while linguistic investigation covers all languages.
D) Verbal definition is a type of definition by genus and differentia, while linguistic investigation is not.

Key Takeaway
Verbal definition is inherently conceptual but can function as assent when the definer intends to confirm a pre-existing word-meaning relation.

Consider the following scenario: In a kalam debate, a speaker defines 'al-`ilm' (knowledge) as 'the attribute by which the knower perceives the known.' Determine whether this verbal definition is primarily conceptual or assertive, and explain your reasoning using the criteria from the passage.

Definition as Pure Conceptualization
Simile of the definer as sculptor and nature of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definition is pure conceptualization without judgment and why scholars prohibit objecting to definitions themselves.

Core Idea
A definition is a sculpted concept, not a judgment; objections belong only to judgments.

The author opens with a powerful simile: the definer is like a sculptor shaping a figure in clay. This illustrates that definition is a pure conceptualization (taswir), containing no judgment (hukm). Since objections (man') are only directed at judgments proven by demonstration, they cannot be aimed at the definition itself. However, definitions come with implicit claims—such as that the definition is proper, comprehensive, and exclusive—which are judgments and can be objected to. Yet scholars have unanimously agreed that objecting to definitions outright is impermissible, to avoid futile dialectics before the real purpose. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Simile
Find the simile in the Arabic: the definer is like a sculptor (naqqash) carving a figure (shabah) in clay (tawh). The simile shows that definition is a creative act of conceptualization, not a logical assertion.
The sculptor analogy emphasizes that definition shapes concepts, not propositions.
2
Extract the Nature of Definition
Notice the phrase 'definition is pure conceptualization, no judgment in it' (al-ta'rif taswirun bahitun, la hukma fihi). This means definitions do not assert truth or falsity; they simply clarify a concept.
This distinguishes definition from propositions (qadaya) which are either true or false.
3
Understand Why Direct Objection is Impossible
The author states that objection (man') only applies to judgments proven by demonstration (burhan). Since a definition is not a judgment, it cannot be directly objected to. Any objection must target the implicit claims attached to the definition.
Implicit claims include: haddiyya (being the true definition), itrad (comprehensiveness), and inkisas (exclusivity).
4
Recognize the Scholarly Consensus
The passage says scholars agree that objecting to definitions as such is absolutely impermissible. The rationale is that the definer can always respond that the definition applies to what he intends, so objection leads to unproductive debate.
This consensus prevents definitional arguments from derailing more important discussions, like building arguments for or against a position.

فصل في المعرف (الثَّالِثُ: مَثَلُ الْمُعَرِّفِ كَمَثَل نَقَّاشِ يَنْقُسُ شَبَحًا فِي التَّوْحِ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَصْوِيرٌ بَحْتُ، لَا حُكْمَ فِيهِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمُنْعِ؛ إذ المنع لا يتوجه إلا إلى الحكم الذي يثبت بالبرهان. نَعَمْ، هُنَاكَ أَحْكَامُ ضِمْنِيَّةٌ، مِثْلَ دَعْوَى الْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْمَنْهُومِيَّةِ وَالِاطْرَادِ وَالِانْعِكَاسِ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، فَيَجُوزُ مَنْعُ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ جوازا عقليا، لَكِنَّ الْعُلَمَاءَ أَجْمَعُوا عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْعَ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لَا يَجُوزُ مطلقا، ولعل وجهه أنه يفضي إلى الجدال قبل الشروع في المقصود الأهم، على أن المعرف لا يعجز عن القول بأن المعرف عندي ما يصدق عليه هذا التعريف، فَكَأَنَّهُ، أي تجويز المنع شَرِيعَةٌ نُسِخَتْ قَبْلَ الْعَمَلِ بِهَا. نَعَمْ، يُنْقَضِ الحد بِإِبْطَالِ الطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْس مثالا. النقض في المشهور مختص بهذين والأشبه جواز النقض بإبطال كل دعوى ضمنية، كالحدية والأعرفية، قيل: المنع يتوجه إلى كل دعاو، سواء كانت معلومة الصحة أو مترددها، فالانتقال عنه إلى النقض غصب المنصب. ولك أن تقول: إن تصور النقض على وجهين: الأول: إبطال مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل باستدلال، الثاني: النقض بالإجمال، فالأول غصب لمنصب المستدل في تلك كرة واحدة، وهو تبين المطلوب بهذه المقدمات، فكان على الخصم منع المقدمة، فالعدول عنه خروج عن منصبه، بخلاف النقض على الوجه الثاني؛ لأن المستدل قد خرج عما التزمه من إقامة الدليل، وعجز الخصم عن القدح مقدمة معينة من مقدماته، فشرع في دعوى أخرى لإبطال الدليل، فافهم. ثم إن التحقيق أن منع غصب المنصب تحكم، بل باطل؛ لأنه منع عن
Chapter on the Definer. (The third): The example of the definer is like a sculptor who carves a figure in clay. So definition is pure conceptualization, with no judgment in it. Therefore nothing of objection is directed against it; for objection is only directed against the judgment that is proven by demonstration. Yes, there are implicit judgments, such as the claim of being a definition, being a notion, and comprehensiveness, and exclusivity, and so on. So it is intellectually permissible to object to those judgments. But the scholars have agreed that objecting to definitions is absolutely not permissible. Perhaps the reason is that it leads to dialectics before embarking on the more important objective, especially since the definer is not incapable of saying that 'the definiendum in my view is whatever this definition applies to.' So it is as if the permission to object is a law abrogated before its implementation. Yes, the definition is refuted by invalidating the comprehensiveness and exclusivity, for example. Refutation in common usage is limited to these two, but the more likely is that refutation is permissible by invalidating every implicit claim, such as being a definition and being more known. It was said: Objection is directed to every claim, whether known to be correct or doubtful, so moving from it to refutation is usurpation of the position. And you may say: The conception of refutation has two aspects: first, invalidating a particular premise of the premises of the proof with a proof; second, refutation in general. The first is usurpation of the position of the one presenting the proof in that one cycle, which is to clarify the sought matter by these premises. So the opponent should have objected to the premise; deviating from that is departing from his position. Unlike refutation in the second way, because the one presenting the proof has departed from what he undertook of establishing the proof, and the opponent is unable to criticize a particular premise of his premises, so he undertakes another claim to invalidate the proof. So understand. Then indeed, the verification is that forbidding objection as usurpation of position is arbitrary, rather it is false, because it is forbidding...
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the nature of definition and the types of objections (man') and refutations (naqd) that can be directed against them. The author begins with a simile: the definer is like a sculptor shaping a figure; definition is pure conceptualization (taswir) without judgment (hukm). Therefore, direct objection (man') is not directed at the definition itself, since objection is only for judgments proven by demonstration. However, implicit claims accompany definitions, such as that the definition is proper (haddiyya), is known (manhumiyya? perhaps 'meaningfulness'), is comprehensive (itrad), and exclusive (inkisas). These implicit claims are judgments, so they can be objected to intellectually. Yet, scholars have agreed that objecting to definitions themselves is absolutely not permissible, because it would lead to dialectics before reaching the main purpose, and the definer can always claim that the definition applies to what he intends. The author notes that the allowance of objection seems to be a law abrogated before its implementation. Then he discusses refutation (naqd). Refutation is commonly restricted to invalidating comprehensiveness and exclusivity, but the more appropriate view is that it can invalidate any implicit claim, like the claim of being a definition or being more known. He then addresses a debate: Some say objection is allowed to any claim, so moving to refutation is usurping the opponent's role. The author clarifies two types of refutation: 1) invalidating a specific premise with a proof (this is usurpation, because the opponent should directly object to that premise), and 2) refutation in general (where the opposing party brings a separate claim because he is unable to attack a specific premise). The second type is legitimate. The passage ends with the author's own verification that forbidding objection as usurpation is arbitrary and false, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
التعريف
المنع
الحدية
الاطراد
الانعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do scholars agree that objecting to definitions is not permissible?
A) Because definitions are not subject to any judgment.
B) Because the definer can always claim the definition applies to his intent.
C) Because objection is only allowed in demonstrative sciences.
D) Because refutation is a better approach.

Key Takeaway
A definition is a conceptual sculpture, not a judgment, so direct objection is impossible; only implicit claims like comprehensiveness can be challenged, but even that is restricted by scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the definition of 'zakat' as 'a specific portion of wealth given to specific recipients' cannot be directly objected to, but its comprehensiveness can be challenged by asking whether it includes all types of wealth.
?
Construct a definition of 'hajj' and identify at least two implicit claims (e.g., comprehensiveness and exclusivity) that could be used to refute it.

Objections and Refutations of Definitions
Distinction between objection and refutation, types of naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between objection (man') and refutation (naqd) of a definition, and apply the two forms of refutation (specific premise vs. general) in a debate scenario.

Core Idea
Refuting a definition means challenging its comprehensiveness or exclusivity, not the definition itself.

After establishing that definitions themselves cannot be directly objected to, the author turns to refutation (naqd), which is the proper way to attack a definition. He explains that refutation is commonly limited to invalidating comprehensiveness (tard) and exclusivity ('aks), but the more appropriate view extends it to any implicit claim. A key debate arises: should the opponent move directly to refutation, or is that usurping the definer's position? The author clarifies two types of refutation—one that attacks a specific premise (which is illegitimate) and one that attacks the overall argument (which is legitimate). This lesson unpacks these distinctions and their application in Islamic scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Man' and Naqd
Man' (objection) is directed only at judgments proven by demonstration. Naqd (refutation) is directed at definitions, but only by challenging their tard (comprehensiveness) or 'aks (exclusivity) or any implicit claim. Note that man' is prohibited for definitions, while naqd is permissible under certain conditions.
The passage says 'the definition is refuted by invalidating the comprehensiveness and exclusivity'.
2
Identify the Two Views on Naqd
Common usage restricts naqd to tard and 'aks. The author suggests a broader view: naqd can invalidate any implicit claim, such as the claim of being a proper definition (haddiyya) or being better known (a'rafiyya).
This broader view gives more flexibility in challenging a weak definition.
3
Analyze the Debate on Usurpation of Position
Some said: Objection is allowed to any claim, so moving from objection to refutation is usurping the opponent's role (ghasb al-manṣab). The author then presents two types of naqd: (1) invalidating a specific premise of the proof (this is usurpation, because the opponent should object directly to that premise), and (2) refutation in general, where the opponent brings a separate claim because he is unable to attack a specific premise (this is legitimate).
The author sides with the second type being valid, and calls the prohibition of the first type arbitrary.
4
Apply to a Debate Scenario
Imagine a mutakallim defines 'ilm (knowledge) as 'certain belief'. The opponent cannot object to the definition itself. Instead, he can challenge its exclusivity: 'Does this definition exclude mere opinion?' That would be a naqd by invalidating 'aks. Alternatively, if the opponent cannot attack a specific premise, he may present a counterexample as a general refutation.
This shows how the tool operates in actual theological discussions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is refutation considered 'usurpation of position' (ghasb al-manṣab)?
A) When the opponent refutes by invalidating a specific premise of the definer's proof.
B) When the opponent refutes by providing a counterexample against tard.
C) When the opponent moves from objection to refutation without permission.
D) When the opponent is unable to attack any specific premise.

Refutation (naqd) of definitions is done by challenging tard or 'aks (or any implicit claim), and it comes in two forms: specific-premise refutation (illegitimate) and general refutation (legitimate when the opponent cannot attack a premise).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about the definition of 'iman' (faith), the opponent refutes by presenting a counterexample: 'Your definition includes all believers but excludes someone who has faith but lacks one condition?' Identify whether this refutation targets tard or 'aks, and whether it is a specific-premise refutation or a general one.
?
Practise a jadal exchange: One person defines 'qiyas' as 'applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a common effective cause'. The other person refutes by invalidating the comprehensiveness: 'But this definition does not cover qiyas al-aks (analogy by reversal).' Decide if this is legitimate naqd and which type it is.

Why Definitions Cannot Be Directly Objected To
Author establishes that definitions are pure conceptualization, immune to direct objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definitions are immune to direct objection and identify the implicit claims that can be challenged.

Core Idea
Definitions are tools of conceptualization, not judgments — objection requires a judgment.

In this passage, the author draws a vivid analogy: a definer is like a sculptor shaping clay — definition is pure conceptualization (tasawwur), free of judgment (hukm). Objection (man‘) is a tool used against judgments, so it cannot directly target a definition. However, definitions carry implicit claims that can be questioned. This lesson unpacks that insight.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the sculptor analogy
Understand that definition is pure conceptualization (tasawwur), not a judgment (hukm). Just as a sculptor shapes clay without making a claim, a definer presents a concept without asserting a truth-claim.
2
Distinguish tasawwur from tasdiq
Recognize that objection (man‘) is directed at judgments (tasdiq) that are proven by demonstration. Since definitions lack judgment, they are not subject to direct objection.
This is a foundational distinction in Islamic logic.
3
Identify implicit claims
Note that definitions carry implicit claims (al-da‘awā al-ḍimniyya) such as comprehensiveness (ṭard), exclusivity (‘aks), being a real definition (ḥaddiyya), and being more known. These claims can be objected to.
The passage lists: دعوى الحدية والمفهومية والاطراد والانعكاس.
4
Understand the scholarly consensus
Scholars unanimously prohibit direct objection to definitions because it leads to disputation (jadal) before addressing the main purpose. The definer can always say the definition is what he intends.
The author compares the permission to object to an abrogated law.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : مَثَلُ الْمُعَرِّفِ كَمَثَلِ نَقَّاشٍ يَنْقُشُ شَبَحًا فِي التَّوْحِ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَصْوِيرٌ بَحْتٌ، لَا حُكْمَ فِيهِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمَنْعِ؛ إذ المنع لا يتوجه إلا إلى الحكم الذي يثبت بالبرهان. نَعَمْ، هُنَاكَ أَحْكَامٌ ضِمْنِيَّةٌ، مِثْلَ دَعْوَى الْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْمَفْهُومِيَّةِ وَالِاطِّرَادِ وَالِانْعِكَاسِ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، فَيَجُوزُ مَنْعُ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ جَوَازًا عَقْلِيًّا، لَكِنَّ الْعُلَمَاءَ أَجْمَعُوا عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْعَ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لَا يَجُوزُ مُطْلَقًا، وَلَعَلَّ وَجْهَهُ أَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إِلَى الْجَدَالِ قَبْلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ الْأَهَمِّ، عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ لَا يَعْجِزُ عَنِ الْقَوْلِ بِأَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ عِنْدِي مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفُ، فَكَأَنَّهُ، أَيْ تَجْوِيزُ الْمَنْعِ شَرِيعَةٌ نُسِخَتْ قَبْلَ الْعَمَلِ بِهَا. نَعَمْ، يُنْقَضُ الْحَدُّ بِإِبْطَالِ الطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْسِ مَثَلًا. النَّقْضُ فِي الْمَشْهُورِ مُخْتَصٌّ بِهَذَيْنِ وَالْأَشْبَهُ جَوَازُ النَّقْضِ بِإِبْطَالِ كُلِّ دَعْوَى ضِمْنِيَّةٍ، كَالْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْأَعْرَفِيَّةِ. قِيلَ: الْمَنْعُ يَتَوَجَّهُ إِلَى كُلِّ دَعْوَى، سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةَ الصِّحَّةِ أَوْ مُتَرَدِّدَةً، فَالِانْتِقَالُ عَنْهُ إِلَى النَّقْضِ غَصْبُ الْمَنْصِبِ. وَلَكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: إِنَّ تَصَوُّرَ النَّقْضِ عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ: إِبْطَالُ مُقَدِّمَةٍ مُعَيَّنَةٍ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتِ الدَّلِيلِ بِاسْتِدْلَالٍ، الثَّانِي: النَّقْضُ بِالْإِجْمَالِ، فَالْأَوَّلُ غَصْبٌ لِمَنْصِبِ الْمُسْتَدِلِّ فِي هَذِهِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَهُوَ تَبْيِينُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِهَذِهِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ، فَكَانَ عَلَى الْخَصْمِ مَنْعُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ، فَالْعُدُولُ عَنْهُ خُرُوجٌ عَنْ مَنْصِبِهِ، بِخِلَافِ النَّقْضِ عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الثَّانِي؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُسْتَدِلَّ قَدْ خَرَجَ عَمَّا الْتَزَمَهُ مِنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ، وَعَجَزَ الْخَصْمُ عَنِ الْقَدْحِ فِي مُقَدِّمَةٍ مُعَيَّنَةٍ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ، فَشَرَعَ فِي دَعْوَى أُخْرَى لِإِبْطَالِ الدَّلِيلِ، فَافْهَمْ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ مَنْعَ غَصْبَ الْمَنْصِبِ تَحَكُّمٌ، بَلْ بَاطِلٌ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَنْعٌ عَنْ إِظْهَارِ الصَّوَابِ، بَلْ نَقُولُ: الْقَدْحُ بِإِبْطَالِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَوْلَى بِالْقَبُولِ مِنَ الْقَدْحِ بِمَنْعِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ الْإِبْطَالَ يَقْطَعُ طَمَعَ إِتْمَامِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الْمَنْعِ، فَافْهَمْ.
The third: The example of the definer is like the example of a sculptor who carves a figure in clay; the definition is pure conceptualization, there is no judgment in it, so nothing of objection is directed at it. Because objection is only directed at the judgment that is proven by demonstration. Yes, there are implicit claims, such as the claim of being a definition, of being understood, of comprehensiveness, of exclusivity, and so on. So objecting to those claims is rationally permissible. But the scholars have unanimously agreed that objecting to definitions is absolutely not permissible. Perhaps its reason is that it leads to disputation before embarking on the more important purpose, especially since the definer is not incapable of saying that the definiendum for me is what this definition applies to. So it is as if the permission to object is a law that was abrogated before acting upon it. Yes, the definition is refuted by invalidating comprehensiveness and exclusivity, for example. Refutation in common usage is confined to these two, but the more likely is the permissibility of refutation by invalidating any implicit claim, such as the claim of definition and of being more known. It was said: Objection is directed at every claim, whether known to be sound or doubtful. So moving from it to refutation is usurpation of position. And you may say: The conception of refutation is in two ways: The first: invalidating a specific premise from the premises of the proof by means of an argument. The second: refutation by summary. The first is usurpation of the position of the proponent regarding these premises, and his position is to clarify the desired conclusion by these premises. So the opponent should object to the premise; deviating from it is leaving his position. Unlike the refutation in the second way, because the proponent has left what he committed to of providing the proof, and the opponent has failed to attack a specific premise from his premises, so he started another claim to invalidate the proof. So understand. Then the verification is that saying that objection is usurpation of position is arbitrary, rather false; because it prevents showing the truth. Rather, we say: Attacking by invalidating a premise is more acceptable than attacking by objecting to it, because invalidation cuts off hope of completing it, unlike objection. So understand.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is the third issue of a chapter on definitions. It addresses the permissibility and limits of objection (man') against definitions. The author opens with an analogy: a definer is like a sculptor carving a figure; definition is pure conceptualization (tasawwur) without judgment (hukm). Therefore, objection, which targets judgments proven by demonstration, cannot directly hit a definition. However, definitions carry implicit claims (ahkam dimniyya) such as that the definition is comprehensive (ittiraad) and exclusive (in'ikaas), that it captures the essence (haddiyya), etc. These implicit claims can be rationally objected to. Yet, scholars unanimously agree that objecting to definitions per se is absolutely not allowed. The reason given is that it would lead to dialectical argument (jadal) before addressing the main subject. Moreover, the definer can always fall back on saying that the definition is what he intends. So the permission to object is like a revealed law that was abrogated before being acted upon. Nevertheless, a definition can be refuted (naqd) by invalidating its comprehensiveness and exclusivity. In common usage, refutation is limited to these two, but the more correct view allows refutation by invalidating any implicit claim. The passage then debates whether objecting to a premise is always the opponent's duty. Some claim that moving from objection to refutation is a usurpation (ghasb) of the proponent's role. The author distinguishes two types of refutation: (1) invalidating a specific premise with a counter-argument, which is usurpation because the opponent should object to the premise, and (2) refutation by summary, where the proponent has failed in his commitment and the opponent cannot pinpoint a premise, so he switches to a different claim. This second type is not usurpation. Finally, the author affirms that the claim that objection is usurpation is arbitrary and false; rather, invalidating a premise is superior to objecting because it permanently removes the proof, whereas objection leaves it open.

Key Terms

النقض
الطرد
العكس
الدعوى الضمنية

Key Takeaway
Definitions are pure conceptualizations; direct objection is impossible, but implicit claims like comprehensiveness and exclusivity can be challenged.

Objection vs. Refutation in Dialectical Debate
Author explains the dialectical relationship between objection and refutation, and refutes the charge of usurpation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between objection (man‘) and refutation (naqd) in dialectical debate, and evaluate the charge of usurpation (ghaṣb al-manṣib).

Core Idea
Refutation by invalidating a premise is superior to mere objection, and the accusation of usurpation is false.

This passage delves into the dialectical rules surrounding definition debates. After establishing that definitions themselves cannot be objected to, the author examines how implicit claims can be refuted. He distinguishes between two types of refutation and tackles the controversial idea that moving from objection to refutation is a usurpation of the opponent's role. The lesson will help you navigate these nuanced rules.

Guided Path
1
Recognize objection vs. refutation
Objection (man‘) rejects a claim without providing a counter-argument; refutation (naqd) invalidates a premise with an argument. The passage discusses when each is appropriate.
2
Understand the common scope of refutation
In common usage, refutation (naqd) of a definition is limited to invalidating its comprehensiveness (ṭard) and exclusivity (‘aks). The author expands it to any implicit claim.
The passage says: النقض في المشهور مختص بهذين والأشبه جواز النقض بإبطال كل دعوى ضمنية.
3
Analyze the charge of usurpation
Some claim that moving from objection to refutation is 'usurpation of position' (ghaṣb al-manṣib) — taking over the proponent's role. The author refines this by distinguishing two types of refutation.
4
Evaluate the author's final verdict
The author concludes that the charge of usurpation is arbitrary and false. Invalidating a premise is actually more decisive than objecting because it cuts off any hope of completing the proof.
The passage: التحقيق أن منع غصب المنصب تحكم، بل باطل... القدح بإبطال مقدمة أولى بالقبول.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : مَثَلُ الْمُعَرِّفِ كَمَثَلِ نَقَّاشٍ يَنْقُشُ شَبَحًا فِي التَّوْحِ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَصْوِيرٌ بَحْتٌ، لَا حُكْمَ فِيهِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمَنْعِ؛ إذ المنع لا يتوجه إلا إلى الحكم الذي يثبت بالبرهان. نَعَمْ، هُنَاكَ أَحْكَامٌ ضِمْنِيَّةٌ، مِثْلَ دَعْوَى الْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْمَفْهُومِيَّةِ وَالِاطِّرَادِ وَالِانْعِكَاسِ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، فَيَجُوزُ مَنْعُ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ جَوَازًا عَقْلِيًّا، لَكِنَّ الْعُلَمَاءَ أَجْمَعُوا عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْعَ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لَا يَجُوزُ مُطْلَقًا، وَلَعَلَّ وَجْهَهُ أَنَّهُ يُفْضِي إِلَى الْجَدَالِ قَبْلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ الْأَهَمِّ، عَلَى أَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ لَا يَعْجِزُ عَنِ الْقَوْلِ بِأَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ عِنْدِي مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفُ، فَكَأَنَّهُ، أَيْ تَجْوِيزُ الْمَنْعِ شَرِيعَةٌ نُسِخَتْ قَبْلَ الْعَمَلِ بِهَا. نَعَمْ، يُنْقَضُ الْحَدُّ بِإِبْطَالِ الطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْسِ مَثَلًا. النَّقْضُ فِي الْمَشْهُورِ مُخْتَصٌّ بِهَذَيْنِ وَالْأَشْبَهُ جَوَازُ النَّقْضِ بِإِبْطَالِ كُلِّ دَعْوَى ضِمْنِيَّةٍ، كَالْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْأَعْرَفِيَّةِ. قِيلَ: الْمَنْعُ يَتَوَجَّهُ إِلَى كُلِّ دَعْوَى، سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةَ الصِّحَّةِ أَوْ مُتَرَدِّدَةً، فَالِانْتِقَالُ عَنْهُ إِلَى النَّقْضِ غَصْبُ الْمَنْصِبِ. وَلَكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: إِنَّ تَصَوُّرَ النَّقْضِ عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ: إِبْطَالُ مُقَدِّمَةٍ مُعَيَّنَةٍ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتِ الدَّلِيلِ بِاسْتِدْلَالٍ، الثَّانِي: Commentary
This passage is the third issue of a chapter on definitions. It addresses the permissibility and limits of objection (man') against definitions. Mu'arada in Real Definitions vs Descriptions
Author explains mu'arada only applies to real definitions, not descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mu'arada in real definitions from descriptions, and evaluate whether a counter-definition constitutes a valid mu'arada or simply a denial.

Core Idea
True mu'arada demands proof from both sides; without it, you're just denying.

In dialectical exchanges about definitions, not every alternative definition counts as a mu'arada. The author clarifies that only real definitions—those capturing a unique essence—are susceptible to genuine counter-objection. Descriptions, which allow multiplicity, cannot be opposed in the same way. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper jadal in definitional contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of definition
Determine whether the definition is a real definition (hadd haqiqi) capturing essence, or a description (rasm) listing accidents. Only real definitions are unique.
Real definitions claim to capture the one true essence.
2
Recognize the requirement for proof
A valid mu'arada requires the opponent to present an alternative definition and provide a proof that it is also a real definition. Without proof, it is not a true mu'arada.
The definer must also have provided proof for his definition.
3
Distinguish between two types of counter-presentations
If the opponent provides proof, it is a genuine mu'arada that refutes the original definition. If no proof is given, it is merely a denial (man') or allowance of another possibility.
Only the former leads to invalidation of the original definition.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic example
Consider definitions of 'salat' in fiqh. If one defines it as 'specific actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' (a real definition), and another says it is 'spiritual connection', the opponent must prove that this alternative captures the essence for it to be a mu'arada.
Without proof, it is not a valid objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ إِنَّمَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْحُدُودِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ، لا في الرسوم؛ إِذْ حَقِيقَةُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا وَاحِدًا، فيعارض [ب]إبداء حد آخر حد المعرف، بِخِلَافِ الرُّسُومِ؛ إذ يجوز فيه التعدد، ثم إن إبداء حد آخر إما بالاستدلال على حديته، وحينئذ يبطل الحد الذي أبدى المعرف؛ لأنه لم يستدل عليه، فهو بالحقيقة نقض بإبطالها، أو إبداء حد آخر من غير إقامة حجة، وهذا لا يصلح للمعارضة، بل قصارى الأمر تجويز حد آخر، وهذا أشبه بالمنع، فالمعارضة في الحد لا يكون إلا إذا أقام المحدد دليلا على دعوى الحدية، وأقام المعارض أيضا دليلا، فافهم.
And the mu'arada is only conceivable in real definitions, not in descriptions; because the essence of a thing is only one, so it is countered by presenting another definition of the thing defined, unlike descriptions, because multiplicity is permissible in them. Then presenting another definition is either by inference upon its being a definition, and then the definition presented by the definer is invalidated because he did not infer upon it, so it is in reality a refutation by invalidating it; or presenting another definition without establishing a proof, and this is not suitable for mu'arada, rather at most it allows another definition, and this resembles a prevention (man'). Therefore, mu'arada in definition only occurs if the definer establishes a proof for the claim of definiteness, and the opponent also establishes a proof. So understand.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mu'arada (counter-objection) in the context of definitions. It begins by stating that mu'arada is only conceivable in real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya), not in descriptions (rusum). The reason given is that the essence (haqiqa) of a thing is unique—there can only be one real definition that captures its essence. Therefore, if someone presents a real definition, a mu'arada can be made by presenting another real definition. In contrast, descriptions can be multiple, so no contradiction arises from multiple descriptions. The author then distinguishes two ways of presenting an alternative definition: either by providing a proof that the alternative is indeed a real definition, which invalidates the original definition because it was not proven, making it a refutation by invalidation; or by presenting an alternative without proof, which does not constitute a valid mu'arada but merely allows the possibility of another definition, similar to a simple denial (man'). Therefore, a proper mu'arada requires both the definer and the opponent to present proofs for their claimed definitions. An Islamic example: If one defines salat as 'specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' (a real definition), and another proposes 'spiritual connection with Allah' as a definition, they must each prove that their definition captures the essence. Without proof, it is not a true mu'arada.

Key Terms
المعارضة
الحدود الحقيقية
الرسوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of definition is mu'arada possible according to this passage?
A) Real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya) only
B) Descriptions (rusum) only
C) Both real definitions and descriptions
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is valid only in real definitions and requires both the definer and the opponent to provide proofs; without proof, it is mere denial.

Compound Meaning and Lexical Definitions
Compound meaning and lexical definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lexical and real definitions by analyzing whether the detail from a compound is part of the defined term's meaning or merely an explanation.

Core Idea
A single word defined by a compound gives only lexical detail, not essential meaning.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle point: when you define a single word using a compound expression (like defining 'zakat' as 'obligatory charity'), the detail in that compound is not part of the meaning of the word itself—it is merely a lexical explanation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper logical definition, especially in Islamic jurisprudence where precise definitions determine legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about single words
The author asserts that a single word does not indicate detail (tafsil) at all. Locate the statement in the Arabic: 'واللفظ المفرد لا يدل على التفصيل أصلا'.
This is the foundational claim for the distinction.
2
Distinguish lexical vs real definition
According to the passage, when a single word is defined by a compound, if the detail is intended, it becomes a real definition; if not, it remains lexical. Find the phrase: 'فلو كان التفصيل مقصودا لانقلب حقيقيا'.
The key condition is whether the detail is part of the intended meaning of the definiendum.
3
Examine the circularity objection
The author presents an objection: if a compound definition provided new meaning, it would cause circularity (dawr) because understanding the word depends on knowing its convention, which depends on knowing the meaning. Identify the argument and its refutation.
The author shows the objection fails for type-based conventions.
4
Understand the response using wad' naw'i
Compound words are established by a type-based convention (الوضع النوعي), so their indication requires knowledge of that convention, not a separate one. This avoids circularity. See: 'فدلالتها أيضا على المعنى بالعلم بالوضع'.
This supports the claim that defining a single word by a compound is only lexical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابع: على معنى مركب فإنما الدلالة عليه من حيث الإجمال والوحدة، وإلا يكن كذلك لجاز تحقق قضية أحادية، أن الملفوظة منحصرة في الثنائية والثلاثية، وقد يستدل بأن دلالة المفرد ليس إلا بالوضع، والوضع الواحد موحد للكثير، وفيه تأمل. واللفظ المفرد لا يدل على التفصيل أصلا، ومن ههنا، أي من أجل أن المفرد لا يدل على التفصيل قالوا: المفرد إذا عرف بمركب تعريفا لفظيا لم يكن التفصيل المستفاد من ذلك المركب مقصودا؛ إذ التعريف اللفظي إنما يدل على ما يدل عليه المعرف من غير فرق ولو اعتبارا، فلو كان التفصيل مقصودا لانقلب حقيقيا. قال الشيخ: الأسماء والكلم في الألفاظ نظير المعقولات المفردة التي لا تفصيل فيها، ولا تركيب ولا صدق ولا كذب، وهو ظاهر بعد ثبوت ما سبق، بل لا يفيد المعنى الغير الحاصل، وإنما منه الإحضار والالتفات فقط، وإلا لزم الدور؛ لأن فهم المعنى من المفرد يتوقف على العلم بالوضع للمعنى، وهو موقوف على تصور المعنى، فلو حصل تصور المعنى به ابتداء لدار. وفيه نظر، أما أولا: فلأن مقدمات الدليل جارية في الألفاظ المركبة، فيلزم أن لا يفيد المعنى أيضا. وأجيب بأن دلالة المركب ليست بوضع على حدة غير وضع الأجزاء، بل الألفاظ المفردة إذا اقترنت على وجه مخصوص فهم معاني تلك المفردات على ذلك الترتيب، فيحصل معنى مركب لم يكن حاصلا من قبل، وهذا خلاف التحقيق؛ فإن الألفاظ المركبة موضوعة بالوضع النوعي، فدلالتها أيضا على المعنى بالعلم بالوضع، كيف ولو كان ترتب المعاني لازما عقليا لترتب الألفاظ كيف لا يفهم المعنى التركيبي من اللفظ المركب من يجهل بأوضاع الهيئات، وإن علم بمعنى المفردات، فافهم. وأما ثانيا: فلأن هذا الدليل لا يتم في الأوضاع النوعية؛ فإن العلم بالوضع النوعي لا يتوقف إلا على العلم بالمعنى بالوجه الكلي، ويكون إفادة المعنى الحاصل بخصوصه، فلا دور، فافهم. فلا يصح التعريف به إلا لفظيا.
The fourth: concerning a compound meaning: the indication of it is from the aspect of summation and unity, and if it were not so, then a single proposition would be possible, that what is uttered is confined to bipartite and tripartite. And it may be argued that the indication of a single word is only by convention, and a single convention unifies many, but there is consideration in it. And a single word does not indicate detail (tafsil) at all. And from this, i.e., because the single word does not indicate detail, they said: when a single word is defined by a compound in a lexical definition, the detail derived from that compound is not intended; because the lexical definition only indicates what the defined term indicates, without distinction even by consideration. For if the detail were intended, it would become a real definition. The Shaykh said: names and words among utterances are analogous to simple intelligibles that have no detail, no composition, no truth and falsehood, and this is apparent after the establishment of what preceded. Rather, it does not convey the meaning that is not yet obtained; it only provides presence and attention, and if not, circularity would follow; because understanding the meaning from a single word depends on knowledge of the convention for the meaning, which depends on conception of the meaning. So if the conception of the meaning were obtained by it initially, circularity would occur. There is consideration in it. As for the first: because the premises of the argument apply to compound words, so it would follow that they too do not convey meaning. And it was answered: the indication of a compound is not by a separate convention apart from the convention of the parts; rather, when single words are combined in a particular way, the meanings of those single words are understood in that order, so a compound meaning is obtained that was not obtained before. But this is contrary to verification; because compound words are established by a type-based convention (wad' naw'i), so their indication of meaning is also through knowledge of the convention. How? If the ordering of meanings were a necessary intellectual consequence of the order of words, then how would anyone ignorant of the conventions of syntactic forms not understand the compound meaning from the compound word, even if they know the meanings of the single words? So understand. And as for the second: because this argument does not hold in type-based conventions; for knowledge of the type-based convention depends only on knowledge of the meaning in a universal aspect, and it provides the meaning obtained in its particularity, so there is no circularity, so understand. So it is not valid to define by it except lexically.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth point in a series about definitions, focusing on compound meaning and lexical definitions. The author begins by stating that a compound meaning is indicated only in terms of summation (ijmal) and unity, otherwise a single proposition would be possible, but utterances are only bipartite or tripartite. The author then notes an argument that a single word indicates only by convention (wad'), and a single convention unifies many meanings; but this is questionable. Next, the author asserts that a single word does not indicate detail (tafsil) at all. Because of this, scholars say that when a single word is defined by a compound in a lexical definition, the detail from the compound is not intended; otherwise it would become a real definition. The author then quotes 'the Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina or a commentator) who analogizes names and words to simple intelligibles that have no detail, composition, truth value. Such a word does not convey a new meaning; it only brings it to mind (ihdar wa iltifat). If it did convey a new meaning, circularity would result: understanding a word depends on knowing its convention, which depends on conceiving the meaning, so if the word itself gave the meaning, there would be a circle. The author then raises two objections: First, the same argument would apply to compound words, implying they too cannot convey meaning. The response is that compound words are not by a separate convention but by combining single words, so the meaning arises from the order. However, the author rejects this response, stating that compound words are established by a type-based convention (wad' naw'i), so their indication also requires knowledge of convention. If meaning followed naturally from word order, anyone knowing the words would understand compounds regardless of grammar knowledge. Second, the argument fails for type-based conventions because knowledge of the type-based convention only requires universal knowledge of the meaning, and it provides the meaning in its particularity, avoiding circularity. Thus, defining a single word by a compound can only be a lexical definition. An Islamic example: Defining 'zakat' as 'a specific obligatory charity at a specific rate' in a lexical definition provides detail, but that detail is not part of the single word's meaning; it only brings the concept to mind. A real definition would capture the essence, not just provide detail.

الوضع النوعي
الدور

Critique of the Cycle Argument in Definition
Commentator critiques the cycle argument in definition by distinguishing compound terms and generic convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the cycle argument against definition by simple terms and explain why it fails for compound terms and generic conventions.

Core Idea
The cycle argument fails because compound terms have a generic convention requiring only universal knowledge.

Why can't simple terms give meaning? Some logicians argued that defining by simple terms leads to a vicious cycle. Our commentator refutes this by distinguishing compound terms and introducing the concept of generic convention (wad' naw'i). In this lesson, we will unpack his critique and see how it safeguards definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cycle argument
Find the claim: understanding a simple term depends on knowing its convention, which depends on conceiving its meaning. If the term gives first-time meaning, a cycle (dawr) results.
2
Apply the objection to compounds
Notice that the same premises would apply to compound terms, but compounds clearly do give meaning. This contradiction suggests the argument is unsound.
3
Understand the response about compounds
The initial response claims compounds indicate meaning through order of parts, not a separate convention. The commentator rejects this, asserting that compounds have a generic convention (wad' naw'i).
4
Examine the generic convention argument
For generic conventions, knowledge of the convention depends only on universal knowledge of the meaning, not prior particular conception. Hence, no cycle arises.
5
Reflect on the counterexample
If meaning were a necessary concomitant of word order, someone ignorant of syntactic conventions would understand compounds. They do not, showing the cycle argument's premises are not universally true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ظاهر بعد ثبوت ما سبق، بل لا يفيد المعنى الغير الحاصل، وإنما منه الإحضار والالتفات فقط، وإلا لزم الدور ; لأن فهم المعنى من المفرد يتوقف على العلم بالوضع للمعنى، وهو موقوف على تصور المعنى، فلو حصل تصور المعنى به ابتداء لدار وفيه نظر، أما أولا: فلأن مقدمات الدليل جارية في الألفاظ المركبة، فيلزم أن لا يفيد المعنى أيضا. وأجيب بأن دلالة المركب ليست بوضع على حدة غير وضع الأجزاء، بل الألفاظ المفردة إذا اقترنت على وجه مخصوص فهم معاني تلك المفردات على ذلك الترتيب، فيحصل معنى مركب لم يكن حاصلا من قبل، وهذا خلاف التحقيق ; فإن الألفاظ المركبة موضوعة بالوضع النوعي، فدلالتها أيضا على المعنى بالعلم بالوضع، كيف ولو كان ترتب المعاني لازما عقليا لترتب الألفاظ كيف لا يفهم المعنى التركيبي من اللفظ المركب من يجهل بأوضاع الهيئات، وإن علم بمعنى المفردات، فافهم. وأما ثانيا: فلأن هذا الدليل لا يتم في الأوضاع النوعية; فإن العلم بالوضع النوعي لا يتوقف إلا على العلم بالمعنى بالوجه الكلي، ويكون إفادة المعنى الحاصل بخصوصه، فلا دور، فافهم.
And it is apparent after establishing what preceded, rather it does not benefit meaning that is not obtained, and rather from it is only bringing to mind and attention, otherwise the cycle would be necessary; because understanding the meaning from a simple term depends on knowledge of the convention for the meaning, and that is dependent on conception of the meaning, so if conception of the meaning were obtained by it initially, there would be a cycle. And there is consideration in it: firstly, because the premises of the argument apply also to compound expressions, so it would necessitate that they also do not give meaning. And it is answered that the indication of a compound is not by a separate convention apart from the convention of its parts; rather, when simple terms are conjoined in a specific manner, the meanings of those simples are understood in that order, resulting in a composite meaning that was not obtained before. And this is contrary to verification; for compound expressions are posited by generic convention (wad' naw'i), so their indication of meaning is also by knowledge of the convention. How? If the ordering of meanings were a necessary concomitance of the ordering of words, why would one who is ignorant of the conventions of the composite forms not understand the composite meaning from the compound expression, even if he knows the meanings of the simples? So reflect. And secondly, this argument does not hold for generic conventions; for knowledge of the generic convention depends only on knowledge of the meaning in a universal aspect, and it gives the specific obtained meaning, so there is no cycle. So reflect.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement where the commentator refutes the argument that defining by simple terms (mufrad) leads to a vicious cycle (dawr). The objection states that if a simple term gave meaning, then understanding its meaning would require prior knowledge of the convention, which in turn requires conception of the meaning, resulting in circularity. The commentator first argues that the same premises apply to compound terms, which would also fail to give meaning if the argument were sound—yet clearly they do. The response distinguishes compound indication: compound terms do not have a separate convention but are understood through the order of their parts, yielding a new composite meaning. This is contrary to the truth that compounds have a generic convention (wad' naw'i). Furthermore, if meaning were a necessary consequence of word order, then someone ignorant of syntactic conventions would still understand, which is false. Secondly, the commentator notes that for generic conventions, knowledge of the convention only requires universal knowledge of the meaning, not prior particular conception, so no cycle occurs. This guards against a misunderstanding that definitions require a regress, reaffirming that simple terms can indeed convey meaning.

Possible vs. Actual Truth: Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on the Subject's Title
States the conventional view and the Farabi–Ibn Sina disagreement on truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-Farabi's possible truth condition from Ibn Sina's actual truth condition for the subject's title, and explain how this affects the interpretation of categorical propositions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Truth of a subject's title can be potential or actual—a fundamental disagreement with big implications.

This passage first establishes what is conventionally accepted in logical consideration regarding 'real individuals', then highlights a pivotal disagreement between two giants of Islamic logic: al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Their dispute centers on the condition for the truth of the subject's title (e.g., 'man') when predicated of its essence (e.g., Zayd). Does the title need to be actually true, or is potential truth sufficient? This distinction has far-reaching implications for how categorical propositions are analyzed and applied in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conventional view (al-muta'araf)
Find in the Arabic what the conventionally accepted interpretation of 'real individuals' is. It is the broader sense (a'amm min al-hissah wa al-fard), not the narrow one exclusive of portions.
This avoids needing metaphorical interpretation for terms like 'existence'.
2
Note the consequence of the conventional view
Understand that by adopting this broader sense, one avoids difficulties in including concepts like 'existence' that would otherwise require special interpretation (ta'wil).
3
Extract al-Farabi's condition for truth of the title
The text states: 'al-Farabi i'tabara sidq 'unwan al-mawdu' 'ala dhatihi bi-l-imkan'. So he accepts the truth if the title is potentially true of the essence.
Potential truth means it holds in all possible instances, even if not actualized.
4
Extract Ibn Sina's condition for truth of the title
The text states: 'wa Ibn Sina bi-l-fi'l'. So he requires actual truth of the title on the essence.
Actual truth requires that the predicate is actually attributed to every existing individual.
5
Compare the two positions and reflect on implications
Consider how this disagreement affects the truth conditions of universal propositions. For example, 'Every human is a writer' would be true for al-Farabi (if humans can potentially write) but false for Ibn Sina (since not all actually write).
This divergence influences how logicians evaluate arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

This difference has implications for the validity and interpretation of categorical propositions.

Understanding Conventional Predication
Author defines conventional predication and illustrates with five and evenness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define conventional predication and identify the three ways a predicate applies to a subject (essential, accidental, abstracted) using the example of 'five is even'.

Core Idea
Conventional predication requires real extramental instantiation, not just mental concepts.

This lesson unpacks the concept of conventional predication (al-ḥaml al-mutaʿārif), the standard type of predication used in Islamic logic. The author clarifies that for a predicate to be true in the conventional sense, it must correspond to a real extramental attribute of the subject. The example of 'five is even' illustrates why conceptual existence alone is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ways a predicate can apply
The passage states three possibilities: (1) the predicate is essential (dhāti) to the subject, (2) it is an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in it, or (3) it is abstracted (muntazaʿ) from it either without relation or with relation.
These categories ensure the predicate genuinely describes the subject extramentally.
2
Understand the criterion: truth of the concept
The criterion for conventional predication is that the concept of the predicate must be true (ṣādiq) of the subject in reality, not merely in the mind.
Mental existence (al-mafhūmāt) is not enough.
3
Analyze the example: five and evenness
The example shows that evenness (zawjiyya) exists conceptually for five, but 'five is even' is false because there is no real five in extramental reality that has evenness abstracted from it.
The instance (miṣdāq) of a five that is even is not realized.
4
Apply the condition to other cases
Use the three ways to test statements: e.g., 'human is rational' (essential), 'Zayd is standing' (accident), 'the ceiling is above' (abstraction with relation).
Practice will solidify the concept.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من تحقيق الحمل وتقسيمه أراد أن يبين مصداق الحمل المتعارف ، وقال: (وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَنْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوع , بِأَنْ يَكُونَ ذَاتِيَّا لَهُ ، أَوْ وَضفَا قَائِمًا بِهِ، أَوْ مُنْتَزَعًا عَنْهُ بِلا إِضَافَةِ أَوْ بِإِضَافَةِ، فَتُبُوتُ زَوْجِيَّةِ الْخَمْسَةِ)؛ بناء على أن المفهومات كلها موجودة في نفس الأمر (لاَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ قَوْلِنَا الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجُ)؛ لعدم تحقق مصداقه، وهو : الخمسة ، بأن ينتزع منه الزوجية.
And when he finished the verification of predication and its division, he intended to clarify the instance of conventional predication. He said: 'The criterion in conventional predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate of the subject, such that it is either essential to it, or an attribute subsisting in it, or abstracted from it without relation or with relation. Thus, the existence of evenness for five does not entail the truth of our statement 'five is even,' because its instance is not realized, i.e., the five from which evenness is abstracted.'
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage clarifies what is meant by 'conventional predication' (al-ḥaml al-mutaʿārif). The author states that for a predication to be true in the conventional sense, the concept of the predicate must be truly applicable to the subject. This applicability can be in three ways: (1) essential (dhāti) to the subject, like 'human is animal'; (2) an accident subsisting in it, like 'Zayd is standing'; or (3) abstracted from it either without relation (like 'Zayd is a writer' from writing) or with relation (like 'the ceiling is above us'). The example of 'five is even' shows that even if evenness exists in the mind as a concept (since all concepts are existent in the mind), it does not necessarily mean that 'five is even' is true in reality because the instance (i.e., a real five that has evenness abstracted from it) is not realized. So conventional predication requires actual extramental instantiation, not merely conceptual existence.

الحمل المتعارف
المفهومات
مصداق
منتزع

A) Because evenness is not a real attribute of five.
B) Because the concept of evenness is not essential to five.
C) Because there is no real five from which evenness can be abstracted.
D) Because 'five is even' is a tautology.

Key Takeaway
Conventional predication demands that the predicate's concept be true of the subject in extramental reality, not merely in the mind.

Conditions for Conventional Predication
Author states the conditions for conventional predication and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for conventional predication and apply them to evaluate a given predication.

Core Idea
Conventional predication requires the predicate to truly apply, not just exist in the mind.

In this passage, the author explains what makes a predication 'conventional' (muta'arif). He gives three ways a predicate can truly describe a subject: as essential (dhati), as a property, or as an abstraction. The example of 'five is even' shows that even if the concept 'even' exists in the mind, it may not truly apply to the subject. This distinction is crucial for logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? Find the term 'al-haml al-muta'arif' (conventional predication) in the Arabic.
2
List the three conditions
The author states that the predicate must be either a dhati (essential), a property (wasf qa'im), or abstracted (muntaza') from the subject (with or without addition). Write down each condition.
Abstracted means the predicate is derived from the subject, like 'evenness' from a number.
3
Understand the counterexample
The author gives the proposition 'the five is even' (al-khamsa zawj). Note why it fails: the concept 'even' exists, but the subject 'five' does not have the property of evenness in reality.
This shows that mental existence of the predicate concept is insufficient.
4
Apply the conditions
Take a simple predication like 'the world is created'. Determine which condition it satisfies. Check if 'created' is dhati, a property, or abstracted for 'world'.
In kalam, 'created' is a property subsisting in the world.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من تحقيق الحمل وتقسيمه أراد أن يبين مصداق الحمل المتعارف ، وقال: (وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَنْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوع ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ ذَاتِيَّا لَهُ ، أَوْ وَضفَا قَائِمًا بِهِ، أَوْ مُنْتَزَعًا عَنْهُ بِلا إِضَافَةِ أَوْ بِإِضَافَةِ، فَتُبُوتُ زَوْجِيَّةِ الْخَمْسَةِ)؛ بناء على أن المفهومات كلها موجودة في نفس الأمر (لاَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ قَوْلِنَا الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجُ)؛ لعدم تحقق مصداقه، وهو : الخمسة ، بأن ينتزع منه الزوجية.
And when he finished from the investigation of predication and its division, he wanted to show the referent of conventional predication, and he said: 'What is considered in conventional predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate of the subject, such that it is either a dhati for it, or a property subsisting in it, or abstracted from it without addition or with addition. So the establishment of 'evenness' of the five — based on the idea that all concepts exist in themselves — does not entail the truth of our statement 'the five is even' because its referent is not realized, namely: the five, by way of abstracting evenness from it.'
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage defines conventional predication (al-haml al-muta'arif). The commentator explains that the predicate must be truly applicable to the subject in one of three ways: as a dhati (essential), as a property (wasf) subsisting in the subject, or as an abstracted attribute (muntaza') either with or without an addition (idafa). Then he gives an example: the concept 'evenness' exists in the mind as a concept, but when predicated of 'five', the proposition 'five is even' is false because the referent (five in reality) does not have the property of evenness. This shows that mere mental existence of the predicate concept does not guarantee the truth of the predication; the predicate must actually apply to the subject in external reality (or in the mind if the subject is mental). The example is from arithmetic: the number five is odd, so 'even' does not apply.

Attributive Combination and Mental Existence
Author explains the attributive combination and its implications for distinguishing mental from external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mental existence from external existence by applying the criterion of necessary effects as derived from the attributive combination.

Core Idea
Mental forms lack the necessary effects of their essences; they exist only in the mind.

This lesson explores the concept of attributive combination (al-ittisaf al-indimami), which requires both attribute and subject to be in the same domain of attribution. Using this principle, the author argues that mental forms have a distinct mode of existence because they do not produce the necessary external effects of their essences. For example, the mental form of fire does not burn, proving it is not externally existent.

Guided Path
1
Define attributive combination
The author defines it as the addition of an attribute to a described thing, requiring both sides (hashiya) to be realized in the same domain—either both externally or both mentally. Identify the two sides: the attribute and the subject.
2
Identify the two considerations of the mental form
Some scholars claim the mental form has two aspects: one as subsisting in the mind with accidents (external) and another as itself (mental). The author challenges this by noting that the particular form and its known are identical.
3
Apply the criterion of necessary effects
The author states that mental existence is known by the absence of necessary effects of the essence. For instance, the mental form of body is not extended, and the mental form of fire does not burn. These mental forms lack the essential properties of their external counterparts.
Necessary effects are the essential accidents that follow from the essence externally.
4
Distinguish mental from external existence
Based on the criterion, anything that does not produce the necessary effects of its essence exists only mentally. Therefore, the attributive combination condition holds: if the attribution were external, the form would have those effects; since it doesn't, the attribution is mental.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic epistemology for separating concepts from realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النكتة (الثَّالِثَةُ : الاِتِّصَافُ الاِنْضِمَامِيُّ)، وهو ما كان بانضمام الصفة إلى الموصوف (يَسْتَدْعِي تَحَقَّقَ الْحَاشِيَتَيْنِ فِي طَرْفِ الاتِّصَافِ)، إِنْ خارجا ففي الخارج وإن ذهنا ففي الذهن، هذا ما هو المشهور، واستدل به بعض أجلة المتأخرين على أن الصورة موجدة في الخارج، وقال: لها اعتباران: اعتبارها من حيث قيامها بالذهن، ومعروضيتها للعوارض، وبهذا الاعتبار موجودة في الخارج لذلك ولترتب الآثار عليها، واعتبارها من حيث هي، مع قطع النظر عن العوارض ، وهي بهذا الاعتبار معلومة وموجودة في الذهن، ولم يحفظ أن الذهن أيضا متصف به ; فإن حلول الشخص مستلزم لحلول الطبيعة، وقد صرح به الفاضل الجونفوري المحمود، وأيضا يترتب عليه الآثار، وهي لوازم الماهية ، فالصورة الشخصية ومعلومها سواء، فما وجه الفرق ؟
وتحقيق المقام أن من المعلوم بالضرورة نحوا من الوجود، وآثارها(۱) غير لوازم الماهية ، ثم بعد التفتيش يظهر نحو آخر من الوجود، بحيث لا يترتب عليه تلك الآثار الضرورية، وإن كانت يترتب عليه آثار أخر ولوازم الماهية كالجسم الغير المتحيز. ولا شك أن الصورة الذهنية لا يترتب عليها آثار ماهياتها ; فإن صورة الجسم غير متحيزة ، وصورة النار غير محزقة ، فيكون موجودة ذهنية، ولا نعني بالموجود الذهني إلا هذا ، وأما مطلق الآثار فعسى (۱) كذا في الأصل، ولعل الصواب: «وآثاره».
The third point: the attributive combination (al-ittisaf al-indimami), which is that which is by the addition of the attribute to the described, requires the realization of both sides (al-hashiyatayn) in the domain of attribution — if externally then in the external, if mentally then in the mind. This is what is well-known. And some eminent later scholars used this to argue that the form (al-sura) is created in the external. They said: it has two considerations: a consideration in terms of its subsistence in the mind, and being a subject for accidents; in this consideration it exists externally for that reason and for the ordering of effects upon it; and a consideration in terms of itself, disregarding accidents, and in this consideration it is known and exists in the mind. And it is not reported that the mind is also qualified by it; for the solution of the particular necessitates the solution of the nature, and that was stated by al-Fadil al-Junfuri al-Mahmud. Also, effects are ordered upon it, and they are the necessities of the essence; so the particular form and its known are the same — what then is the difference? The investigation of the matter is that it is known by necessity that there is a mode of existence, and its effects (1) are not the necessities of the essence. Then after examination, another mode of existence appears, such that those necessary effects are not ordered upon it, although other effects and necessities of the essence — like the body that is not extended — are ordered upon it. And there is no doubt that the mental form does not have the effects of its essence ordered upon it; for the form of body is not extended, and the form of fire does not burn. So it is a mental existent, and we do not mean by 'mental existent' except this. As for absolute effects, perhaps (1) so it is in the original, and perhaps the correct reading is 'and its effects'.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical analysis of the concept of 'attributive combination' (al-ittisaf al-indimami), which refers to the addition of an attribute to a described thing. The author states that this combination requires both sides (the attribute and the subject) to be realized in the same domain — if the attribution is external, both must be external; if mental, both mental. This is the well-known view. The author then discusses how some later scholars used this principle to argue that the mental form (al-sura al-dhihniyya) actually exists externally. They claimed the form has two considerations: one as subsisting in the mind and being subject to accidents (in which case it is external because it has external effects), and another as itself disregarding accidents (in which case it is mental). The author challenges this by noting that the particular form and its known (the same form as known) are identical, so why claim a difference? The resolution is that there is a mode of existence that is known by necessity, whose effects are not the necessities of the essence. After investigation, another mode appears that does not produce those necessary effects but does produce other effects and necessities of the essence. For example, the mental form of a body is not extended, and the mental form of fire does not burn. Therefore, it is a mental existent, and that is all that is meant by 'mental existent'. The author leaves the question of absolute effects open.

حاشية
صورة ذهنية

C) Because fire cannot exist in the mind.
D) Because the mind cannot imagine fire.

Key Takeaway
The attributive combination requires both attribute and subject to be in the same domain, and mental forms lack the necessary effects of their essences, proving their existence is only mental.
