وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ) بعد تصور الطرفين، (وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا ، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٌ ظَاهِرٍ)، من السمع والبصر والذائقة واللامسة والشامة ، (وَهِيَ الْحِرِّيَّاتُ ، أَوْ بِحِسٌ بَاطِنٍ(۲)) ، لعله أراد به قوة سوى الحس الظاهر لتدخل قوة العاقلة للنفس، (وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ (۳) ، وَمِنْهَا) ، أي بعض الوجدانيات الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ). (۱) قال ابن سينا في الإشارات (٣٥٠/١): هي قضايا، إنما يصدق فيها لأجل الوسط، لكن ذلك الوسط ليس مما يعزب عن الذهن، فيحوج فيه الذهن إلى طلب، بل كلما أخطر حد المطلوب بالبال خطر الوسط بالبال ، مثل قضائنا بأن الاثنين نصف الأربعة». (۲) قال السعد في شرح العقائد النسفية (٢٦): وأما الحواس الباطنة التي تثبتها الفلاسفة فلا تتم دلائلها على الأصول الإسلامية»، قال المولى الخيالي معلقا عليه (٢٦): «فإنها مبنية على أن النفس لا تدرك الجزئيات المادية بالذات، وعلى أن الواحد لا يكون مبدأ لأثرين ، والكل باطل في الإسلام». (۳) قال صاحب المواقف (١٢٤/١): إنها قليلة النفع في العلوم ؛ لأنها غير مشتركة، فلا تقوم حجة على الغير». وقال شارح المواقف (١٢٤/١): الحسيات والبديهيات هما العمدة في العلوم ، وهما يقومان حجة على الغير. الصناعات الخمس يسمى الوهم قوة في الدماغ تدرك المعاني الجزئية التي لا يدركها الحواس، كالعداوة في الذئب، والعُطُوفة في الأب، وما يصدق بهذه الحاسة وهميات، والوهم سلطان ،القوى، له تصرف على سائر المعقولات، حتى إنه ربما يعارض العقل، ويوقعه في الغلط، فالتصديق الذي يتعلق بالمحسوسات بواسطة الوهم يكون صادقا مطابقا ، كحكمه بأن في الذئب عداوة، وبأن الكرة إذا تحركت على نفسها أحدثت من حركتها كل نقطة دائرة، فهذا حكم العقل بواسطة الوهم صادق والتصديق الذي تعلق بغير المحسوسات بوساطته يكون كاذبا ، كحكمه بأن كل موجود في مكان ، فالقسم الأول من الوجدانيات . (وَمِنْهَا (١) مَا نَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا لَا بِآلَاتِنَا من أحوال النفس والآلات، كالحكم بأن القضية ليس فيها نسبة سوى النسبة التامة الحاكية. فلعلك تفطنت بأن المشاهدات على ثلاثة أصناف، الأول ما يدرك بالحواس الظاهرة، الثاني ما يدرك بالباطن ، ومنها الوهميات في المحسوسات الثالث ما يدرك نفوسنا، والأخيران يسميان وجدانيات(٢). ثم ليس كل تصديق يحصل بالحواس من المشاهدات، بل لا بد فيها من حكم العقل أيضا وقبولها، وإلا لكان قولنا للس راب إنه ماء يصير من المشاهدات ، وكذا سائر أغلاط الوهم والحس هذا ، والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب العليم الخبير. (۳) وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيَّا)؛ فإن الحس لا يدرك إلا
And the innate (fitriyya) propositions: they are what need a middle term that does not leave the mind, after the two extremes are conceived. They are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observational (mushahada) [propositions] are either by external sense, like hearing, sight, taste, touch, and smell, and they are the sensory (hissiyyat), or by internal sense, and they are the intuitive (wijdaniyyat). And among them (i.e., some of the wijdaniyyat) are the estimative (wahmiyyat) in sensed things. [Footnote 1: Ibn Sina said...] [Footnote 2: al-Sa'd said...] [Footnote 3: Sahib al-Mawaqif said...] The five arts. Estimation (wahm) is called a power in the brain that perceives particular meanings not perceived by the senses, like enmity in the wolf and affection in the father. And what is believed by this sense are estimative propositions. Wahm is the sultan of the powers; it has influence over all intelligibles, so much so that it sometimes opposes reason and causes it to err. The assent that relates to sensed things through wahm is true and conforming, like its judgment that there is enmity in the wolf, and that when a sphere moves on its axis, each point of its motion traces a circle. This judgment of reason through wahm is true. And the assent that relates to non-sensed things through it is false, like its judgment that every existent is in a place. So that is the first division of the wijdaniyyat. And among them (i.e., the wijdaniyyat) is what we find in ourselves without instruments, from states of the soul and instruments, like the judgment that the proposition has no relation except the complete informative relation. Perhaps you have realized that the observational [propositions] are of three types: first, what is perceived by the external senses; second, what is perceived by the internal [sense], and among them are the wahmiyyat in sensed things; third, what is perceived by ourselves. The last two are called wijdaniyyat. Then not every assent obtained by the senses is from the observational; rather, it must also include a judgment of reason and its acceptance. Otherwise, our statement 'the mirage is water' would become observational, and similarly other errors of wahm and sense. This, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the unseen, the All-Knowing, the All-Aware. (3) The truth is that sense does not give except a particular judgment; for sense only perceives...
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory lesson. It first defines fitriyyat (innate propositions) as those requiring a middle term that never leaves the mind after the extremes are conceived; they are called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them' (i.e., the middle term is always present). Then it divides mushahedat (observational propositions) into those by external senses (hissiyyat) and those by internal sense (wijdaniyyat). The commentator provides footnotes quoting Ibn Sina's definition of fitriyyat, al-Sa'd and al-Khayali's critique of internal senses from an Islamic perspective, and al-Mawaqif's remark that wijdaniyyat are of little use in the sciences because they are not shared. The passage then explains wahm (estimation), a power in the brain that perceives particular meanings not sensed (e.g., enmity in a wolf). Wahm can be true when applied to sensed things (e.g., judging enmity in a wolf) but false when applied to non-sensed things (e.g., judging that every existent occupies place). It then clarifies that wijdaniyyat include not only wahmiyyat but also internal experiences like the judgment that a proposition has only a complete informative relation. The passage summarizes that mushahedat are of three types: external sense perceptions, internal sense perceptions (including wahmiyyat in sensed things), and what we perceive in ourselves (without instruments). The last two are wijdaniyyat. Finally, it warns that not every sense-based assent is observational; reason must also judge, otherwise mirage would be considered water. The passage ends with a note that sense gives only particular judgments (partial, not universal). This classification is crucial for understanding the foundations of certainty in logic and the limits of sensory knowledge, especially in Islamic theology where wahm is often critiqued.

Key Terms
الوهم
الوهميات
الحواس الباطنة
حكم جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When it judges about sensed things like the enmity in a wolf
B) When it judges about non-sensed things like the location of all existents
C) When it relies on external senses
D) When it is combined with fitriyyat

Key Takeaway
Wahm yields true knowledge only about sensible particulars; applying it beyond that leads to error, and sense knowledge in general is limited to particular judgments.

Definition of Hadsiyyat and Distinction from Fikr
Author defines intuitive judgments and contrasts with discursive thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intuitive (hads) and discursive (fikr) reasoning and identify intuitive judgments as a type of premise in demonstration.

Core Idea
Intuition grasps ordered premises in a flash, while thought climbs them step by step.

This passage defines a special type of logical premise: intuitive judgments (hads). The author not only defines what intuition is but also contrasts it sharply with discursive reasoning. Understanding this distinction is crucial because intuitive judgments are considered certain and can serve as starting points for demonstration. Let's see how the mind works in each case.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of hads
Find the phrase 'sunuh al-mabadi' al-murattaba daf'atan' and understand each element: 'occurrence', 'principles', 'ordered', 'suddenly'.
2
Contrast with fikr
Identify the key verb 'yatadarraju' (proceeds gradually) vs. 'yantaqil... daf'atan' (moves instantly). Note the step-by-step sequence in fikr: conceptualizing the goal, acquiring principles, formulating propositions, selecting, arranging.
3
Recognize the mechanism of intuition
In intuition, the mind jumps from the sought (matlub) directly to the ordered premises. This implies the soul can attend to two propositions simultaneously.
4
Note the epistemological implication
Intuition does not require repeated observation; it is immediate. This makes hadsiyyat a type of certain knowledge (yaqiniyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ)، أي الحدس (سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِئِ الْمُرَتَبَةِ دَفْعَةً) . وفرقوا بين الحدس والفكر أن (۱) في الفكر يتدرج الذهن بعد تصور المطلوب في تحصيل المبادئ، فيحصل القضايا، ثم يأخذ منها ما يناسبه، فيرتب كذلك ، أي تدريجا فيحصل المطلوب. وفي الحدس ينتقل الذهن من المطلوب إلى المبادئ المرتبة دفعة ، إما بعد تعب أو لا ، فيحصل المطلوب عقيبه دفعة. وهذا يرشدك إلى أن النفس قد تلتفتُ في آن واحد إلى قضيتين). والعلم الحقيقي المطابق عند العليم الخبير. (وَلَا يَجِبُ) في الحدس (الْمُشَاهَدَةُ) مرة، (فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا ، كَمَا قِيلَ (۳)) في شرح الإشارات».
(And the intuitive judgments, and they are), i.e., intuition (is the sudden occurrence of ordered principles). And they distinguished between intuition and discursive thought: that (1) in discursive thought, the mind proceeds gradually after conceptualizing the sought to acquire the principles, then it obtains the propositions, then it takes from them what is suitable, and arranges them accordingly, i.e., gradually, and then it obtains the sought. And in intuition, the mind moves from the sought to the ordered principles instantly, either after exertion or not, and the sought is obtained immediately thereafter. And this guides you to the fact that the soul may turn in a single moment to two propositions. And true knowledge that corresponds (to reality) is with the All-Knowing, the Aware. (And observation is not necessary) in intuition once, (let alone its repetition, as was said (3)) in Sharh al-Isharat.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage defines hadsiyyat, which are intuitive judgments, as a type of premise in logic. The term hads (intuition) is defined as 'the sudden occurrence of ordered principles' – meaning that the mind grasps the logical relationships between premises and conclusion in a flash, without step-by-step reasoning. The author contrasts this with fikr (discursive thought), where the mind gradually goes through steps: first conceptualizing the goal, then acquiring principles, formulating propositions, selecting suitable ones, and arranging them sequentially until the conclusion is reached. In intuition, the mind leaps directly from the sought (matalub) to the ordered principles in an instant, either after some prior mental exertion or without it. This demonstrates that the soul can attend to two propositions simultaneously. The passage notes that intuition does not require repeated observation, as is stated in Sharh al-Isharat. This is important because intuitive judgments are considered certain and can serve as principles in demonstration.

Key Terms
الحدس (al-hads): Intuition: the sudden occurrence of ordered principles in the mind, leading to immediate acceptance of a conclusion.
الفكر (al-fikr): Discursive thought: a gradual process of moving from premises to conclusion through sequential reasoning.
سنوح المبادئ (sunuh al-mabadi): The occurrence of principles in the mind; here 'principles' refer to the premises of a syllogism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does intuition (hads) differ from discursive thought (fikr) according to the passage?
A) Intuition is slower than discursive thought.
B) Intuition grasps ordered principles instantly, while discursive thought proceeds gradually.
C) Intuition requires repeated observation, while discursive thought does not.
D) There is no difference; they are the same process.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive judgments (hads) are premises grasped instantly in an ordered manner, setting them apart from the gradual process of discursive thought (fikr).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Fifth Art: Sophistry (al-Safsata) — Definition and Purpose
Author defines the fifth art (safsata) and illustrates with examples of wahmiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sophistry (safsata) from general fallacy (mughalata) and identify its defining feature: composition from wahmiyyat.

Core Idea
Sophistry is not just any false argument — it is a crafted deception using imaginative premises that mimic self-evident truths.

In the classification of the five logical arts, the fifth is sophistry (al-safsata). This passage defines it precisely: sophistry is a faculty for constructing arguments from imaginative premises (wahmiyyat) that appear true to the imagination but are not rationally certain. The purpose is either to test an opponent or to avoid such fallacies. The author also clarifies that sophistry is a specific species of the broader genus 'fallacy' (mughalata). Understanding this distinction is crucial for guarding against deceptive reasoning in Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. The author begins with 'الْخَامِسُ : السَّفْسَطَةُ' — the fifth art is sophistry.
The term is السفسطة.
2
Extract the genus
What category does the author place sophistry in? He calls it a 'ملكة' (faculty) and 'صناعة' (craft), i.e., a disciplined ability to compose arguments.
Genus: ملكة (faculty) or صناعة (craft).
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes sophistry? It is composed of 'الوهميات' (imaginatives) — premises that seem true to the imagination but are not rationally certain.
The author gives an example: 'كل موجود مشار إليه' (Every existent is pointed to).
4
Understand the purpose
Why would someone use sophistry? The purpose is 'تغليط الخصم امتحانًا' (to mislead the opponent as a test) or to avoid such syllogisms (الاجتناب).
The purpose distinguishes it from honest argumentation.
5
Distinguish from mughalata
How does sophistry relate to the general term fallacy? The author states 'والمغالطة أعم' — fallacy is more general, covering any argument invalid in form or matter.
Sophistry is a specific type of fallacy, not all fallacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : السَّفْسَطَةُ، وَهُوَ الْمُؤَلَّفُ مِنَ الْوَهْمِيَّاتِ نَحْوُ كُلُّ مَوْجُودٍ مُشَارٍ إِلَيْهِ، وَالنَّفْسُ مُسَخَّرَةٌ لِلْوَهْمِ وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ، فَالْوَهْمِيَّاتُ رُبَّمَا لَمْ تَتَمَيَّزْ عِنْدَهَا مِنَ الْأَوَّلِيَّاتِ، وَلَوْلَا دَفْعُ الْعَقْلِ حُكْمَ الْوَهْمِ بَقِيَ الِالْتِبَاسُ دَائِمًا، أَوْ مِنَ الْمُشَبَّهَاتِ بِالصَّادِقَةِ صُورَةً أَوْ مَعْنًى، كَأَخْذِ الْخَارِجِيَّاتِ مَكَانَ الذِّهْنِيَّاتِ وَبِالْعَكْسِ. وَصِنَاعَةُ السَّفْسَطَةِ مَلَكَةٌ يُقْتَدَرُ بِهَا عَلَى تَأْلِيفِ حُجَجٍ سَفْسَطِيَّةٍ، وَالْغَرَضُ مِنْهُ تَغْلِيطُ الْخَصْمِ امْتِحَانًا، أَوِ الِاجْتِنَابُ عَنْ هَذِهِ الْأَقْيِسَةِ. وَالْمُغَالَطَةُ أَعَمُّ، فَإِنَّهَا الْفَاسِدَةُ صُورَةً أَوْ مَادَّةً.
The fifth: Sophistry, and it is composed of imaginatives, like 'Every existent is pointed to', and the soul is subservient to the imagination and what pertains to it. So the imaginatives perhaps are not distinguished by it from the self-evidents. And were it not for the intellect repelling the judgment of imagination, confusion would remain always; or it is from the equivocations resembling true ones in form or meaning, like taking external things in place of mental ones and vice versa. The craft of sophistry is a faculty by which one is able to compose sophistical arguments, and its purpose is to mislead the opponent, either as a test or to avoid such syllogisms. And fallacy is more general, for it is that which is invalid in form or matter.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth logical art: sophistry (safsata). The author begins by stating that sophistry is composed of imaginative premises (wahmiyyat), giving an example: 'Every existent is pointed to' — a claim that seems plausible to the imagination but is not rationally necessary. He explains that the soul is naturally inclined to the imagination, and imaginative judgments can be mistaken for self-evident truths (awwaliyyat). Only reason can correct this, otherwise confusion persists. Alternatively, sophistry may consist of arguments that resemble true ones in form or meaning but are not, such as substituting external (khariji) premises for mental (dhihni) ones. He then defines sophistry as a faculty (malaka) for constructing deceptive arguments, with the purpose of either testing an opponent or avoiding such fallacies. Finally, he clarifies that 'fallacy' (mughalata) is broader than sophistry, encompassing any invalid argument in form or matter.

Key Terms
السفسطة
الوهميات
المغالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Safsata is a faculty for poetry, while mughalata is for rhetoric.
D) Safsata is only used for teaching, while mughalata is used for debate.

Key Takeaway
Sophistry is a distinct logical art that uses imaginative premises to deceive; it is a specific type of fallacy, not every fallacy.

How General Predicates Become Essential Accidents
Author explains how predicates that are more general can be considered essential accidents when qualified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a more general predicate functions as an essential accident when restricted, using the examples of equality in geometry and arithmetic.

Core Idea
A general predicate becomes essential when restricted to the subject's nature.

In this passage, the author explains a subtle point from Ibn Sina: predicates that are more general than the subject can still be considered essential accidents, provided they are suitably qualified. This resolves a puzzle in how we investigate attributes in the mathematical sciences. We'll walk through the examples of 'equality' in geometry and arithmetic to see how a simple restriction transforms a general predicate into an essential accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general predicate
Find the predicate that is more general than the subject in the passage. For example, 'equality' is a general concept that applies to both magnitudes and numbers, making it more general than the subject of geometry (magnitudes) or arithmetic (numbers).
General predicates can belong to multiple subjects.
2
Recognize the need for restriction
Note that the predicate alone is not an essential accident. It must be restricted by a specific condition to become per se to the subject. The author says 'this accident is restricted by a restriction, so it becomes an essential accident for the subject'.
The restriction ties the predicate to the subject's nature.
3
Apply the restriction to geometry
For geometry, equality is restricted to 'the two magnitudes being such that one can be superimposed on the other'. This specific sense of equality follows from the nature of magnitudes, making it an essential accident of geometry's subject.
The restriction must be intrinsic to the subject.
4
Apply the restriction to arithmetic
For arithmetic, equality is restricted to 'the two numbers containing units in a single number'. This sense follows from the nature of numbers, making it an essential accident of arithmetic.
Different restrictions yield different essential accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه قال الشيخ قد يكون محمولات الصناعتين أعم من الموضوعات، عوارض ذاتية للجنس، كالمساواة في علم الهندسة، والعدد، وعوارض ذاتية لما يشبه الجنس، كالقوة والفعل في العلم الطبيعي. ومعنى ذلك أنه يقيد هذا العارض بقيد، فيصير عرضا ذاتيا للموضوع، فيبحث فيه، فالمساواة إذا فسرت بكون المقدارين بحيث يصح انطباق أحدهما على الآخر يصير عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع الهندسة، فيبحث فيه . وإن فسرت بكون العددين مشتملين على الوحدات بعدة واحدة كان عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع الحساب. وهذا مصرّح في مواضع في «الشفاء»، وليس الأمر كما زعم المتأخرون، من أنه يصح البحث عما يعرض الموضوع بواسطة جزئه
Then know that the Shaykh said: The predicates of the two arts (geometry and arithmetic) may be more general than the subjects, being essential accidents of the genus, like equality in the science of geometry, and number, and essential accidents of what resembles the genus, like power and act in natural science. And its meaning is that this accident is restricted by a restriction, so it becomes an essential accident for the subject, and it is investigated therein. For equality, if interpreted as the two magnitudes being such that one can be superimposed on the other, becomes an essential accident for the subject of geometry, and it is investigated therein. And if interpreted as the two numbers containing units in a single number, it becomes an essential accident for the subject of arithmetic. This is explicitly stated in places in the Shifa, and the matter is not as the later scholars claimed, that it is permissible to investigate what is accidental to the subject by means of its part
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a doctrine from Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) regarding predicates that are more general than the subject in the two arts of geometry and arithmetic. The author explains that such predicates can be considered essential accidents when they are suitably qualified. For example, 'equality' as a general predicate can be an essential accident for the subject of geometry when interpreted as the superimposability of magnitudes, and for arithmetic when interpreted as the equality of numbers in terms of units. The author stresses that this is clearly stated in Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, contrary to the claim of later scholars who allowed investigating what is accidental to the subject via its part. The passage thus reinforces the correct understanding of how general predicates can be per se accidents.

Key Terms
محمول
عارض ذاتي
صناعتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is taken without any restriction.
D) It is considered as a genus.

Key Takeaway
A more general predicate becomes an essential accident for a subject when it is restricted to a specific condition inherent to that subject.

Classification of Principles (Mabadi')
Author classifies principles into common and specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the types of principles (mabadi') in demonstration according to the author's classification.

Core Idea
Principles are either common or specific; specific principles further divide into definitions, self-evident truths, assumed principles, and postulates.

The author concludes his logical treatise by explaining what principles are and how they function as the foundation of demonstration. He presents a detailed classification that helps us understand the different roles principles play in various sciences. This structure is essential for any seeker of knowledge who wants to ensure their reasoning rests on solid ground.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Look for the phrase 'إما عامة وإما خاصة'. Common principles apply to all sciences (e.g., law of non-contradiction). Specific principles belong to one or two sciences.
Common principles are usually omitted due to their obviousness.
2
Distinguish conceptual and propositional specific principles
If the specific principle is a concept, it is called a definition (hadd); if it is a proposition, proceed to the next steps.
3
Subdivide propositional principles into necessary and theoretical
Necessary propositions are self-evident (common knowledge); theoretical propositions must be conceded in the science but proved elsewhere.
Otherwise they become questions (masa'il) rather than principles.
4
Further subdivide theoretical propositions
If accepted by the learner due to probability or trust, they are assumed principles (usul mawdu'a); if accepted despite denial, they are postulates (musadarat). Note that sometimes these two are not distinguished.
5
Apply the qualification for common principles in two sciences
When a common principle is used in two sciences, it must be qualified with a restriction to become specific to each.
The author mentions an example was just given (not in this excerpt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمبادئ من الوسائل، المبادئ ما يتألف منه البرهان، وهي إما عامة يستعمل في جميع العلوم على السواء، نحو «اجتماع النقيضين محال» وارتفاعهما محال». وقد جرت العادة بتركها في العلوم؛ لشهرتها وجلائها. وإما خاصة بعلم أو علمين، فإن كانت من قبيل التصورات فتسمى حدودا، وإن كانت قضايا فإما ضرورية غنية عن البيان، فتسمى علوما متعارفة، وإما نظرية، فلا بد أن تسلّم في العلم الذي هذه المبادئ مبادئ له، ويُبيَّن في علم آخر، وإلا لصارت مسائل. فإن كانت بحيث يقبلها المتعلم؛ إما لكونها مظنونة، أو لحسن ظنه فتسمى أصولا موضوعة، وإن كانت بحيث سلمها مع إنكار فتسمى مصادرات، وربما لا يفرّق ويسمى الأخيران أصولا موضوعة. والمبادئ المشتركة في علمين لا بد لمقيد الصناعة أن يقيد حين الاستعمال بقيد تكون به مختصة بذلك العلم، ومثاله قد آنفا. والأحسن والأليق لكل علم أن يذكر مبادئه التصورية والتصديقية أجمع صدر العلم أو صدر كل باب ما يليق به؛ ليأمن المتعلم عن الغلط، وقد حافظ عليه أهل الهندسة والحساب وسائر الرياضيين، ولذلك ما ترى في كتبهم غلطا، ولم يحافظ عليه أهل الطبيعي والفلسفة، ولذا ترى في كتبهم غلطا أكثر. والله يقول الحق ويهدي السبيل، الحمد لله الذي وفق عبده أبا العياش عبد العلي محمدا فصَّ ختام هذا الكتاب بالاختتام، والصلاة على سيد الأنام محمد وآله الكرام، وأصحابه العظام، اللهم اجعل سيئاته متقلبة بالحسنات، واجعل حبيبك محمدا عليه وآله الصلاة شفيعا [له] يوم يقوم الحساب، وانفع العباد.
And principles are among the means. Principles are that from which demonstration is composed. They are either common, used in all sciences alike, such as 'the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible' and 'their removal is impossible'. And custom has been to leave them in the sciences due to their fame and clarity. Or they are specific to one or two sciences. If they are of the category of concepts, they are called definitions; if they are propositions, then either necessary (dharuriyya) and need no explanation, and they are called common knowledge; or they are theoretical, so they must be conceded in the science for which these principles are principles, and explained in another science, otherwise they would become questions. If they are such that the learner accepts them—either because they are probable or due to good opinion—they are called assumed principles; if they are such that he concedes them while denying, they are called postulates. And perhaps no distinction is made, and the last two are called assumed principles. Common principles in two sciences must be qualified by the practitioner with a restriction when used, so that they become specific to that science; its example has just been given. And the best and most fitting for every science is to mention its conceptual and assertoric principles together at the beginning of the science or at the beginning of each chapter what is appropriate, so that the learner may be secure from error. The people of geometry, arithmetic, and the rest of the mathematicians have maintained this, and therefore you do not see error in their books; whereas the people of natural philosophy and philosophy did not maintain it, and therefore you see more error in their books. And God speaks the truth and guides the way. Praise be to God who enabled His servant Abu al-'Ayash 'Abd al-'Ali Muhammad to complete the conclusion of this book. And peace be upon the master of mankind, Muhammad, and his noble family and great companions. O God, make his evil deeds transformed into good deeds, and make Your beloved Muhammad, peace be upon him and his family, an intercessor for him on the Day of Reckoning, and benefit the servants.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological conclusion. It defines 'principles' (mabadi') as the components from which demonstration (burhan) is constructed. The author classifies principles into two types: common principles (like the law of non-contradiction) which are so well-known they are usually omitted in sciences, and specific principles that belong to one or two sciences. Specific principles are further divided: if they are concepts, they are called definitions (hudud); if they are propositions, they are either necessary (self-evident) called 'common knowledge', or theoretical, which must be accepted in the science and proved in another. Among theoretical propositions, if accepted by the learner due to probability or trust, they are 'assumed principles' (usul mawdu'a); if accepted despite denial (skeptically), they are 'postulates' (musadarat). Sometimes these two are conflated. The author then advises that common principles shared between two sciences must be qualified with a restriction to make them specific when used. He praises the mathematicians for stating their principles upfront, which prevents error, contrasting them with natural philosophers and philosophers who neglect this, leading to more errors. The passage ends with a colophon praising God and praying for the author's forgiveness and the benefit of believers.

Key Terms
المبادئ
البرهان
الأصول الموضوعة
المصادرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes assumed principles (usul mawdu'a) from postulates (musadarat)?
A) Assumed principles are probable or accepted due to good opinion, while postulates are conceded despite initial denial.
B) Assumed principles are common to all sciences, while postulates are specific.
C) Assumed principles are necessary, while postulates are theoretical.
D) There is no distinction; the author uses them interchangeably.

Key Takeaway
Principles of demonstration are systematically classified into common and specific, with specific principles further divided into definitions, self-evident truths, assumed principles, and postulates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the principle 'the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible' is common or specific. Then, find a specific principle from the science of tafsir (e.g., 'the apparent meaning of the Quran is authoritative') and classify it according to the author's scheme.

Methodological Advice and Conclusion
Author advises to state principles at the beginning of each science and concludes with a colophon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mathematicians' books contain fewer errors according to the author, and apply this advice to any Islamic science.

Core Idea
Explicitly stating all principles at the beginning of a science prevents error; mathematicians do this, philosophers neglect it.

After presenting the classification of principles, the author offers practical advice: every science should state its principles upfront at the beginning. He contrasts the meticulous practice of mathematicians with the negligence of natural philosophers, showing the consequences. The passage concludes with a colophon praising God and praying for the author and readers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's recommendation
Look for 'والأحسن والأليق لكل علم أن يذكر مبادئه التصورية والتصديقية أجمع صدر العلم'. The best practice is to mention all conceptual and assertoric principles at the start.
2
Understand the consequence of following vs. neglecting
Mathematicians maintain this practice, so their books are free from error; philosophers neglect it, so their books contain more error.
The author uses this as empirical evidence for the effectiveness of the method.
3
Apply the principle to an Islamic science
Consider how a science like tafsir could benefit from stating its principles (e.g., principles of interpretation) at the outset.
4
Reflect on the colophon
The author ends with a prayer for forgiveness and intercession. This reminds us that the ultimate goal of knowledge is to draw closer to Allah.

Classification of Principles in the Sciences
Author classifies principles in the sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given principle as a conceptual definition (hadd), a self-evident axiom (ulum muta'arifa), a posited principle (usul mawdu'a), or a postulate (musadara), based on the criteria in the passage.

Core Idea
Every science rests on principles, and their correct classification is the first step to sound reasoning.

In this concluding passage, the author provides a detailed classification of the principles (mabadi') that form the foundations of any science. Understanding this classification is essential because it tells us how different types of principles should be treated: some need proof, some are self-evident, and others are accepted provisionally. We will walk through the classification step by step so you can apply it to real examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the principle is conceptual or propositional.
The passage first divides principles into two broad categories: conceptual (tasawwuri) and propositional (tasdiqi). Conceptual principles clarify the essences of things and are called definitions (hudud). Propositional principles are statements that can be true or false.
For example, a definition like 'man is a rational animal' is conceptual; a statement like 'the world is originated' is propositional.
2
For propositional principles, check if they are self-evident or theoretical.
If the proposition is necessary and needs no explanation, it is called 'ulum muta'arifa (self-evident axioms). If it is theoretical, it requires proof, but that proof must come from another science; within its own science it is accepted as a principle.
An example of a self-evident axiom: 'The whole is greater than its part.' A theoretical principle: 'The world is originated' is proven in kalam but accepted as a principle in physics.
3
For theoretical principles, determine how they are accepted.
If the learner accepts the principle due to its plausibility or trust in the teacher, it is called usul mawdu'a (posited principles). If the learner accepts it despite initial denial or opposition, it is called musadarat (postulates). Often the two are not distinguished.
For instance, in fiqh, the principle 'qiyas is a valid source of law' may be accepted as usul mawdu'a by a student who trusts the jurist, or as a musadara in a debate with someone who denies it.
4
Apply the classification to a real example.
Take the principle: 'Every effect has a cause.' Is it conceptual or propositional? Propositional. Is it self-evident? In many philosophical traditions, yes, so it belongs to ulum muta'arifa. If a student doubts it, it might be treated as usul mawdu'a in a pedagogical setting.
This step reinforces the classification by requiring you to use the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة للكتاب وارتفاعهما محال». وقد جرت العادة بتركها في العلوم ؛ لشهرتها وجلائها. وإما خاصة بعلم أو علمين، فإن كانت من قبيل التصورات فتسمى حدودا، وإن كانت قضايا فإما ضرورية غنية عن البيان، فتسمى علوما متعارفة ، وإما نظرية ، فلا بد أن تسلّم في العلم الذي هذه المبادئ مبادئ له، ويُبيَّن في علم آخر، وإلا لصارت مسائل . فإن كانت بحيث يقبلها المتعلم ؛ إما لكونها مظنونة، أو لحسن ظنه فتسمى أصولا موضوعة وإن كانت بحيث سلمها مع إنكار فتسمى مصادرات ، وربما لا يفرّق ويسمى الأخيران أصولا موضوعة. والمبادئ المشتركة في علمين لا بد لمقيد الصناعة أن يقيد حين الاستعمال بقيد تكون به مختصة بذلك العلم، ومثاله قد آنفا . والأحسن والأليق لكل علم أن يذكر مبادئه التصورية والتصديقية أجمع صدر العلم أو صدر كل باب ما يليق به؛ ليأمن المتعلم عن الغلط، وقد حافظ عليه أهل الهندسة والحساب وسائر الرياضيين، ولذلك ما ترى في كتبهم غلطا، ولم يحافظ عليه أهل الطبيعي والفلسفة، ولذا ترى في كتبهم غلطا أكثر . والله يقول الحق ويهدي السبيل، الحمد لله الذي وفق عبده أبا العياش عبد العلي محمدا فص ختام هذا الكتاب بالاختتام، والصلاة على سيد الأنام محمد وآله الكرام، وأصحابه العظام، اللهم اجعل سيئاته متقلبة بالحسنات، واجعل حبيبك محمدا عليه وآله الصلاة شفيعا له يوم يقوم الحساب، وانفع بهذا الكتاب كما تنفعت بمنته ؛ إنك أنت سميع الدعاء ، آمين . والمرجو ممن ينتفع بهذا الكتاب أن لا ينساني من الدعاء، وأسأله الله .
Conclusion of the book: 'and their rising is impossible.' And it has been the custom to omit them in the sciences due to their fame and clarity. Or they are specific to one science or two sciences. If they are of the type of conceptions, they are called definitions (hudud). If they are propositions, then either they are necessary and need no explanation, and they are called common sciences (ulum muta'arifa), or they are theoretical, so they must be accepted in the science for which these are principles, and are explained in another science; otherwise they would become problems. If they are such that the learner accepts them, either due to their being probable or due to good opinion, they are called posited principles (usul mawdu'a). If they are such that they are accepted despite denial, they are called postulates (musadarat). And perhaps one does not distinguish, and the two latter are called posited principles. Common principles in two sciences must be restricted by the practitioner with a restriction that makes them specific to that science, and its example has been given recently. And the best and most appropriate for each science is to mention its conceptual and propositional principles all together at the beginning of the science or at the beginning of each section what is appropriate for it, so that the learner is safe from error. And the people of geometry, arithmetic, and all mathematicians have observed this, therefore you see no error in their books. And the people of natural science and philosophy have not observed this, therefore you see more error in their books. And Allah says the truth and guides to the way. Praise be to Allah who enabled His servant Abu al-'Ayash 'Abd al-'Ali Muhammad to seal the conclusion of this book with completion. And blessings upon the master of mankind Muhammad and his noble family and great companions. O Allah, turn his evil deeds into good deeds, and make Your beloved Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, an intercessor for him on the Day of Reckoning. And benefit through this book as You benefited through its author; indeed You are the Hearer of supplication, Amen. And the request from whoever benefits from this book is that he not forget me in supplication, and I ask Allah.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the book. It continues the discussion of principles (mabadi') in the sciences. The author first mentions a common practice: certain principles are left out of books due to their fame. Then he divides principles into those specific to a science or two. If they are conceptual (tasawwuri), they are called definitions (hudud). If they are propositional (tasdiqi), they are either necessary and self-evident—called common sciences (ulum muta'arifa)—or theoretical, which need to be accepted within the science but proven elsewhere. If accepted on the basis of plausibility or trust, they are called posited principles (usul mawdu'a); if accepted despite objection, they are called postulates (musadarat). Sometimes these two are not distinguished. The author then discusses common principles between two sciences: they must be restricted to the specific science when used. He recommends that every science state its conceptual and propositional principles at the outset, to prevent error. He notes that mathematicians follow this and have fewer errors, while natural philosophers do not and have more errors. The passage ends with a prayer and request for supplication.

مبادئ
حدود
علوم متعارفة
أصول موضوعة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes 'usul mawdu'a' from 'musadarat'?
A) Usul mawdu'a are self-evident, while musadarat are theoretical.
B) Usul mawdu'a are accepted due to probability or good opinion, while musadarat are accepted despite initial denial.
C) Usul mawdu'a are conceptual, while musadarat are propositional.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Principles in a science are either conceptual definitions or propositions; propositions are further divided into self-evident axioms, posited principles accepted on trust, and postulates accepted despite objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following principles according to the passage: (a) 'The whole is greater than the part' (used in geometry); (b) 'Qiyas is a valid source of Islamic law' (used in usul al-fiqh); (c) 'The world is originated' (used in kalam and physics). For each, state whether it is conceptual or propositional, self-evident or theoretical, and if theoretical, whether it is likely usul mawdu'a or musadara.
?
Take a principle from an Islamic science of your choice (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of Allah' in usul al-fiqh or kalam) and explain which category it falls into based on the author's criteria.

Stating Principles to Prevent Error
Author recommends stating principles at the start of each science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why explicitly stating all principles at the outset of a science prevents error, and apply this recommendation to an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Mathematicians avoid error by listing all their principles upfront; natural philosophers suffer errors because they neglect this practice.

The author concludes his book with a practical observation: the best way to protect learners from error is to state all conceptual and propositional principles at the beginning of a science (or at the beginning of each section). He contrasts the success of mathematicians, who do this, with the errors found in the books of natural philosophers. In this lesson, we will analyze this recommendation and see how it applies to Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about mathematicians and natural philosophers.
The author states that mathematicians (ahl al-handasa, al-hisab, and all riyadiyyun) consistently state their principles at the start, and therefore their books contain no errors. In contrast, natural philosophers (ahl al-tabi'i) and philosophers (ahl al-falsafa) do not, so their books contain more errors.
This is an empirical observation based on the author's study of these fields.
2
Explain why stating principles prevents error.
When a learner knows all the foundational definitions and axioms upfront, they can check whether subsequent reasoning is consistent with those principles. Without explicit principles, hidden assumptions can lead to mistakes.
This is related to the classification lesson: clear principles provide a solid foundation.
3
Consider an example from an Islamic science that does not always state its principles.
For instance, in tafsir (Quranic exegesis), there are many principles (e.g., 'the general is interpreted in light of the specific', 'the apparent meaning is preferred'). If these are not stated at the beginning of a commentary, the reader may misinterpret verses.
In contrast, works of fiqh often begin with a section on usul to clarify principles.
4
Apply the recommendation to a discipline of your choice.
Take the science of hadith (mustalah al-hadith). What are its foundational principles? Should they be stated at the start of every book on the topic? The author would say yes, to prevent errors in evaluating hadith.
Think about how this principle could improve your own study.

خاتمة للكتاب وارتفاعهما محال». وقد جرت العادة بتركها في العلوم ؛ لشهرتها وجلائها. وإما خاصة بعلم أو علمين، فإن كانت من قبيل التصورات فتسمى حدودا، وإن كانت قضايا فإما ضرورية غنية عن البيان، فتسمى علوما متعارفة ، وإما نظرية ، فلا بد أن تسلّم في العلم الذي هذه المبادئ مبادئ له، ويُبيَّن في علم آخر، وإلا لصارت مسائل . فإن كانت بحيث يقبلها المتعلم ؛ إما لكونها مظنونة، أو لحسن ظنه فتسمى أصولا موضوعة وإن كانت بحيث سلمها مع إنكار فتسمى مصادرات ، وربما لا يفرّق ويسمى الأخيران أصولا موضوعة. والمبادئ المشتركة في علمين لا بد لمقيد الصناعة أن يقيد حين الاستعمال بقيد تكون به مختصة بذلك العلم، ومثاله قد آنفا . والأحسن والأليق لكل علم أن يذكر مبادئه التصورية والتصديقية أجمع صدر العلم أو صدر كل باب ما يليق به؛ ليأمن المتعلم عن الغلط، وقد حافظ عليه أهل الهندسة والحساب وسائر الرياضيين، ولذلك ما ترى في كتبهم غلطا، ولم يحافظ عليه أهل الطبيعي والفلسفة، ولذا ترى في كتبهم غلطا أكثر . والله يقول الحق ويهدي السبيل، الحمد لله الذي وفق عبده أبا العياش عبد العلي محمدا فص ختام هذا الكتاب بالاختتام، والصلاة على سيد الأنام محمد وآله الكرام، وأصحابه العظام، اللهم اجعل سيئاته متقلبة بالحسنات، واجعل حبيبك محمدا عليه وآله الصلاة شفيعا له يوم يقوم الحساب، وانفع بهذا الكتاب كما تنفعت بمنته ؛ إنك أنت سميع الدعاء ، آمين . والمرجو ممن ينتفع بهذا الكتاب أن لا ينساني من الدعاء، وأسأله الله .
Conclusion of the book: 'and their rising is impossible.' And it has been the custom to omit them in the sciences due to their fame and clarity. Or they are specific to one science or two sciences. If they are of the type of conceptions, they are called definitions (hudud). If they are propositions, then either they are necessary and need no explanation, and they are called common sciences (ulum muta'arifa), or they are theoretical, so they must be accepted in the science for which these are principles, and are explained in another science; otherwise they would become problems. If they are such that the learner accepts them, either due to their being probable or due to good opinion, they are called posited principles (usul mawdu'a). If they are such that they are accepted despite denial, they are called postulates (musadarat). And perhaps one does not distinguish, and the two latter are called posited principles. Common principles in two sciences must be restricted by the practitioner with a restriction that makes them specific to that science, and its example has been given recently. And the best and most appropriate for each science is to mention its conceptual and propositional principles all together at the beginning of the science or at the beginning of each section what is appropriate for it, so that the learner is safe from error. And the people of geometry, arithmetic, and all mathematicians have observed this, therefore you see no error in their books. And the people of natural science and philosophy have not observed this, therefore you see more error in their books. And Allah says the truth and guides to the way. Praise be to Allah who enabled His servant Abu al-'Ayash 'Abd al-'Ali Muhammad to seal the conclusion of this book with completion. And blessings upon the master of mankind Muhammad and his noble family and great companions. O Allah, turn his evil deeds into good deeds, and make Your beloved Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, an intercessor for him on the Day of Reckoning. And benefit through this book as You benefited through its author; indeed You are the Hearer of supplication, Amen. And the request from whoever benefits from this book is that he not forget me in supplication, and I ask Allah.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the book. It continues the discussion of principles (mabadi') in the sciences. The author first mentions a common practice: certain principles are left out of books due to their fame. Then he divides principles into those specific to a science or two. If they are conceptual (tasawwuri), they are called definitions (hudud). If they are propositional (tasdiqi), they are either necessary and self-evident—called common sciences (ulum muta'arifa)—or theoretical, which need to be accepted within the science but proven elsewhere. If accepted on the basis of plausibility or trust, they are called posited principles (usul mawdu'a); if accepted despite objection, they are called postulates (musadarat). Sometimes these two are not distinguished. The author then discusses common principles between two sciences: they must be restricted to the specific science when used. He recommends that every science state its conceptual and propositional principles at the outset, to prevent error. He notes that mathematicians follow this and have fewer errors, while natural philosophers do not and have more errors. The passage ends with a prayer and request for supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for the lack of errors in mathematicians' books?
A) They use more rigorous mathematics.
B) They state all their principles at the beginning.
C) They avoid using theoretical principles.
D) They copy from each other.

Key Takeaway
Stating all principles explicitly at the outset of a science is the best safeguard against logical errors in reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Choose an Islamic science (e.g., hadith criticism, fiqh, kalam) and list at least three of its foundational principles that should be stated at the beginning of a textbook on that science. Explain how stating each principle could prevent a specific type of error.
?
Find a passage from an Islamic text (e.g., a work of kalam like al-Sanusiyya) that states its principles at the start. Write a short paragraph explaining how the author's recommendation from this lesson is illustrated by that text.
